==PAGE:3==
Summary (English)
The goal of this thesis is to develop a graphical domain specic language to be
used with the RobustRailS project. This is used to extend an existing textual
domain specic language, such that a graphical denition is available. Further-
more an environment is to be implemented that supports the graphical language.
In order to achieve this, several approaches on how to visualize a network-layout,
both statically and dynamically is presented. These approaches are compared,
and the best approach is chosen as the solution.
Dierent frameworks for developing domain specic languages, are studied to de-
termine which will be best, when we have to implement our graphical language.
Two frameworks are studied, namely MetaEdit+ and EMF/GMF. Advantages
and disadvantages of both are discussed, such that the best suited framework is
chosen, for implementing the environment.
After the framework has been chosen, a design for an environment is provided,
that enables one to implement the graphical domain specic language that has
been dened. This design will provide functionality, that enables for easy ex-
tendibility, such that new elements may be added after deployment. This is as
the textual domain specic language may be extended with new features.
As the environment is to be implemented with the use of the Eclipse Modelling
Framework and Graphical Modelling Framework, it enables one to get an editor
generated as a plug-in for Eclipse, that incorporates the provided domain model.
This generated plug-in will be customized to t the needs of the environment.
Ultimately, an environment has been created, that supports the developed
graphical domain specic language, as well as having extendibility incorporated,
allowing for deployment of new types of elements without altering code.

Summary (Danish)
Målet for denne afhandling er at a udvikle et grask domæne specikt sprog
der skal bruges i sammenhæng med RobustRailS projektet. Dette vil udvide et
allerede eksisterende tekstbaseret domæne specikt sprog, så det har en grask
denition til rådighed. Ydermere vil et miljø blive implementeret, der under-
støtter det graske sprog.
For at opnå dette, vil vi gennemgå ere metoder der kan udnyttes til at vi-
sualisere et netværk, som indeholder både en statisk og dynamisk adfærd. De
foreslåede muligheder vil blive sammenlignet, og den bedste metode vil blive
brugt til at udvikle miljøet.
Forskellige programmerings platforme til udvikling af domæne specikke sprog
vil blive undersøgt, for at bestemme hvilket vil fungere bedst i udviklingen af
det graske sprog. To programmering platforme vil blive undersøgt, hvilket er
MetaEdit+ og EMF/GMF. Både fordele og ulemper ved at bruge disse plat-
forme vil blive gennemgået, så den bedste programmering platform er valgt til
udviklingen af miljøet, der skal understøtte det graske sprog.
Efter valget af programmering platform, vil et design for miljøet der skal un-
derstøtte det grask domæne specikke sprog, blive fremlagt. Dette design vil
indeholde funktionalitet der understøtter nem udvidelse, så man således kan til-
føje nye elementer til miljøet. Dette gøres så man kan understøtte potentielle
ændringer i det tekstbaseret domæne specikke sprog.
Miljøet er blevet implementeret ved brug af Eclipse Modelling Framework og
Graphical Modeling Framework, som gør at en editor bliver genereret som et
plug-in til Eclipse. Dette plug-in understøtter domæne modellen for det domæne
specikke sprog, og bliver tilpasset for at understøtte kravene til miljøet.
Som slutprodukt, vil et miljø der understøtter det graske domænse specikke
==PAGE:6==
iv
sprog være implementeret, som understøtter at nye elementer kan tilføjes, uden
der er behov for at ændre på koden for implementationen.
==PAGE:7==
Preface
This thesis was prepared at the department of Informatics and Mathematical
Modelling at the Technical University of Denmark under supervision of As-
sociate Professor Anne E. Haxthausen, in fullment of the requirements for
acquiring an M.Sc. in Informatics.
The thesis deals with the development of a graphical domain specic language
that is to be used within the RobustRailS project, to model and verify the
correctness of interlocking systems.
The project period for the thesis was from September 2nd, 2014 to February
2nd, 2015.
Lyngby, 02-February-2015
Andreas Foldager

==PAGE:15==
Chapter 1
Introduction
The overall goal of this project is to provide an user-friendly environment for a
tool-set, that can be used for verifying the safety of the future Danish interlock-
ing systems. In this chapter we will give a motivation for this, explaining the
goal of the project, and at last give an overview of the structure of the report.
1.1
Motivation
The signalling system on the Danish railways system is to be replaced with the
standardised European Train Control System (ETCS) Level 2. And with this,
the interlocking system will have to replaced as well. The task of the interlocking
system is to control the dynamic allocation of routes for trains and the track-side
equipment (e.g. points), such that trains can travel safely through the network
without colliding with each other or derailing.
As the interlocking systems are to be replaced, we must ensure that the routes
are safe, to which the RobustRailS research project, has as one of its goal to
develop a holistic method to verify the correctness of the given routes. Currently
this is integrated into a tool-chain, in which one has to make a description of a
network (currently in XML). From this description a formal model and safety
properties are generated, using a generator tool.
A model checker is lastly
==PAGE:16==
2
Introduction
applied to the generated model and properties, and veries whether they are
correct, and supplies counter-examples in case the conguration is not safe.
Overall the creation of a network description, as an XML le is currently very
cumbersome and not very user-friendly. This is due to the fact, that one can eas-
ily lose track of what has already been dened, and what needs to be connected,
especially when the network gets larger.
1.2
Goal
The goal of this project is thus to create a user-friendly environment, that
supports the creations of such networks, and display counter-examples in case
the verication fails.
In order to establish this user-friendly environment, the existing domain specic
language, must have a graphical domain specic language specied, that com-
plies with the domain. Moreover it should ensure that illegal networks may not
be established.
Finally, the environment must function with the existing tool-chain, such that
it will act as a front-end to the tool-chain  meaning it will have creation of the
network and representation of the counter-examples incorporated into a single
environment.
The developed environment, should support extendibility, such that a developer
may with ease add new elements to the environment.
See Appendix A for the actual problem statement.
1.3
Chapter Overview
In the report, we will provide knowledge of the process of developing the graph-
ical domain specic language, and implementation of such a language in an en-
vironment. This is done in a linear approach, as seen by the chapters included
in the report.
Chapter 2 provides an analysis of the existing domain, including the static and
dynamic behaviour of the elements contained within the domain. Further-
==PAGE:17==
1.3 Chapter Overview
3
more similar editors, that is used to construct network-layouts is examined,
and requirements for the environment to be developed is listed.
Chapter 3 will provide a small description of what a domain specic language
is, and which frameworks may be utilized in the development of such
languages. A discussion on which framework to use for the development
is conducted, resulting in a framework being chosen as the framework to
be used for the development.
Chapter 4 describe the visual aspects that is to be included within the graph-
ical domain specic language. This includes both the static and dynamic
features of each element contained within the domain. Furthermore, ap-
proaches on how to construct an editor, that support this domain is dis-
cussed.
Chapter 5 provides an overview of some of the most common work-ows, that
will occur within the environment.
Chapter 6 explain the design behind the environment, including the domain
model, which is the basis of the environment, as well as several general
decisions that was taken in regards to the design of the environment.
Chapter 7 highlights some of the implementation details about the environ-
ment, which is mostly related to the usage of EMF/GMF.
Chapter 8 highlights the current state of the environment, showing what has
been achieved, both in regards to the simulation, but also in relation to
how it may be extended for future use.
Chapter 9 is a discussion about what features could be included in the envi-
ronment to improve the usability.
==PAGE:18==
==PAGE:19==
Chapter 2
Analysis
Before describing anything related to the solution, it would be nice to have an
idea, what already exists on the market, that can deal with the issue at hand.
This is done to see if any tool can be utilized for the environment, and otherwise
just get some ideas on what others have done within the given domain.
The domain will likewise be described in detail, to get the context that we shall
base the environment on. This include both what has already been constructed
for the RobustRailS project, and the domain that the environment should extend
upon.
Lastly formal requirements for the environment to be developed will be listed,
such that we have features, that must be implemented in the nal environment
for it to be considered a success.
2.1
RobustRailS
RobustRailS is an interdisciplinary project, with the purpose of discovering
whether we can get trains on time [1]. This is crucial for the transportation
of people going by train, as it may potentially save them precious time  as it
==PAGE:20==
6
Analysis
enables them to schedule their travels accordingly. Furthermore, RobustRailS
wants to achieve a higher throughput on the train-networks, allowing more trains
to travel on the network at any given time.
RobustRailS, was put in motion to verify these properties for the new signalling
system, that is to be deployed in Denmark.
This new signalling system, that will be deployed in the period 2009-2021 com-
plies with the standardized European Train Control System (ETCS) level 2 [2].
One crucial part of this new signalling systems, is the interlocking systems, that
will ensure that all dynamically allocated train routes are to be fullled with-
out violating any safety properties. In order to verify that no such violations
will happen in the interlocking systems, RobustRailS has as one of its goals
to develop a holistic method, that in turn will support the verication of the
interlocking systems based on the safety properties.
2.1.1
Domain Specic Language
The holistic method, that is used to verify the interlocking systems, has its
own domain specic language (DSL). This has been dened primarily to ease
communication between the dierent actors that is part of the development, i.e.
domain experts, software engineers, and certication authorities [2].
The given DSL is used to describe the interlocking system, and it operational
environment (the railway network).
2.1.1.1
Specication in DSL
A specication (interlocking system) is the top most element in the DSL, and
consists of a single network layout and an interlocking table [2].
Figure 2.1 shows the current DSL relation between the dierent elements con-
tained in a specication. A solid arrows indicates that the element which the
connection originates from contain the referenced element, while a white arrow
indicates that it is a specialization of the referenced element.
Network Layout
A network layout describes the dierent track-side elements
that is available within a network.
Currently there exist three categories of
==PAGE:21==
2.1 RobustRailS
7
Point
Linear
Section
Markerboards
Level Crossings
Route
Interlocking Table
Network
Specification
Figure 2.1: Existing Domain Specic Language
elements, sections, markerboards and level crossings [2]. Each element has a
unique ID.
Sections
The sections may be two dierent types, namely a linear or point.
Currently all sections has a length and a number of neighbours, that is also
sections.
The linear section describes a single line of tracks of various lengths, and the
DSL has abstracted away from having curves occurring on a linear section. A
linear section has at most two neighbours, one in the UP direction and one
in the DOWN direction. UP and DOWN are used in Denmark to describe in
which direction a train moves in regards to a reference point.
For the point section, it has at most three neighbours, one for the stem, one
for the plus and nally one for the minus end. The stem is a lone end in the
opposite direction of plus and minus. The plus and stem, may be seen as a
linear section, as they represent the straight sub-section of the point. The last
end, namely the minus, is the point that diverts away from this linear section
established by the stem and plus, and is what enables trains to divert their route
onto another stretch of sections.
The way a point, determines whether a train should move towards the plus or
minus, is determined by the positioning of the point. The position of a point can
take three positions, namely PLUS, MINUS or INTERMEDIATE. And when it
is in the PLUS position, train move from the stem to the plus or vice versa. And
in case the position of the point is MINUS instead, trains would move towards
the minus end instead of the plus.
If the point is in the INTERMEDIATE
position, no trains should be on the point, as it might cause a derailing [3].
==PAGE:22==
8
Analysis
Do note that a section does not need to have the given amount of neighbours,
as they may represent an end of the overall network layout.
Markerboards
A markerboard has a reference to the section that it is lo-
cated at, and in which end it is placed (based on the direction UP or DOWN ).
Currently a markerboard may only be placed on linear sections. Furthermore, it
has a distance assigned, which states how far from the given end it is located [2].
In ETCS Level 2, there are no physical signals, instead it has virtual signals
attached to the markerboards throughout the network. A virtual signal may
take the values OPEN or CLOSED, indicating whether it allow or disallow trains
to pass [3].
Level Crossings
Finally, we have level-crossings, which in the current state
of the DSL only has a set of parallel sections that is crosses [2]. Do note that
this may not be the nal conguration for a level-crossing, as they are still
under development.
Interlocking Table
The interlocking table is what the interlocking system
uses to reserve portions of the physical network, when a train needs to go from
one point to another. In order for the interlocking system to reserve the needed
sections in the network, it utilizes routes.
A route is a collection of track-side elements contained within a specic network.
Stated in [2] [3], a route currently consists of:
(1) A source, given as a markerboard
(2) A destination, given as another markerboard. The source and destination
must be placed in the same direction, i.e. UP or DOWN.
(3) A path of all sections from the source to the destination
(4) A set of sections that act as overlap on the route, establishing protection
zones in case the train overshoots its path.
(5) The points of the route, and their required position (either PLUS or MI-
NUS).
(6) A set of markerboards, acting as front- and ank-protection for the route.
==PAGE:23==
2.1 RobustRailS
9
(7) Set of conicting routes, which may not be active while the given route is
active.
Each route is identied by an unique ID, such that one can reference it from
other routes.
To avoid safety violations, routes are exclusive, meaning only a single train may
utilize a given route at any given time. When used, a route gets locked avoiding
any other trains from getting the route granted [3].
The interlocking table then contains several routes, that may be used by trains
to traverse the network that the interlocking table is related to.

any given interlocking system. The modelling process of using this tool-chain
can be seen in Figure 2.2

Figure 2.3: Network-layout, that the snippet in Listing 2.1 describes a small

However with the XML le describing the DSL, it will get parsed to an internal
representation, that is utilized in the rest of the tool-chain. However before the
model-checker veries the correctness, the given specication must comply to the
static semantic rules dened for the DSL. These rules are checked with a static-
checker, that informs whether the provided specication is in fact wellformed.
In case it is not wellformed, the errors will be provided as output. And if it is
wellformed, the tool-chain may continue with the verication.
As an option, the interlocking table generator (ITG) may be used to generate
routes for the network, described in the XML le.
In case the given specication was wellformed, a model instance will be gen-
erated from a generic model, describing the dynamic behaviour of the Danish
interlocking system. This model instance will be given as a Kripke structure[4].
At the same time, concrete safety properties are also generated by taking the
given specication, and running them through the dened generic safety-pro-
perties.
Finally, the generated model instance will then be veried against the generated
safety properties, utilizing bounded model checking and inductive reasoning. In
case that the given model instance does not comply with the safety proper-
ties, counter examples will be generated.
These counter-examples will show
the modeller the steps (a trace of consecutive states), that leads to an unsafe
state [3].

(in case one does not utilize the ITG), is time-consuming and cumbersome.
This is one of the main reasons for creating a graphical front-end to create the
network-layout and corresponding interlocking table in an easy and fast way.

2.2
Dynamic Behaviour of the Domain
The static behaviour of the DSL, has already been described in Section 2.1.1,
however the DSL also has a dynamic behaviour associated with the dierent
elements.
As mentioned, we have four dierent elements, that a network can consist of,
linear sections, point sections, markerboards and level-crossings. Most of
==PAGE:27==
2.2 Dynamic Behaviour of the Domain
13
these have dynamic behaviour dened for them, in relation to some properties,
that is used to determine which state the given element should be in.
The four types of entities described, can furthermore be split into two cate-
gories, namely Sections and Additions/Extensions. The latter describes all the
additional features that may be associated with the track sections of a network,
e.g. a Markerboard.
2.2.1
Sections
These are the entities that describes the outline of the Network, i.e. where the
trains may move.
2.2.1.1
State Encoding
Any Section in the network has a state encoded, such that one can locate any
issues that may arise. Currently, all Sections has some features in common,
which are primarily used to determine if any trains are present on the Section.
HTO-value
Value, containing 3-bits that describes how a train is occupying
a given direction on a track section. The three bits is the value are:
H
T
O
The meaning of these bits in the HTO-value is:
• (H) indicates that the front of a train is present in the direction that the
value represents.
• (T) indicates that the tail of a train is present in the direction that the
value represents, and
• (O) indicates that the Section is occupied by a train.
So when a bit in the HTO-value is set to 1, it indicates that the portion of
the section, represented by the bit, is occupied.
By having this value, the
DSL abstracts away from knowing the exact location, that a train has on a
given section  but instead focus on where the individual parts of the trains are
located within the network.
==PAGE:28==
14
Analysis
MODE
Describes the state of the track section, i.e. whether it is occupied
or free. For the current implemented track sections, it may take the following
values:
• FREE(0): The value when the section is not used by a route in any way
 meaning a route may try to get permission to use it.
• EXLCK(1): Means that the section is being reserved by a route  making
it ready to be used for the route.
• USED(2): Indicates that a route is using the section.
The transaction diagram, depicting how a Section, may change its MODE, de-
pendent on which value it currently has, can be seen in Figure 2.5. As seen, a
Sections MODE-value has a circular pattern for how it behaves.
Due to the MODE-value, a Section may be referred to as a lockable element, as
a route may lock a Section for itself.
FREE
EXLCK
USED
Figure 2.5: Transition diagram for the lockable elements
PREV
Is used to determine whether the previous section in the route cur-
rently using the section has been released. Used to determine whether the
section may be released  when using sequential releasing. More about the
usage of this value, can be seen in Section 2.2.3.1.
2.2.1.2
Linear Section
The Linear Section describes a long stretch of rail tracks, that contains two
entry points. In the current state, we abstract away from curved sections, mean-
ing all sections are considered to be straight.
As a result of being straight, a Linear Section must have at least one, and at
most two neighbouring sections, that it is connected to. This means that it
==PAGE:29==
2.2 Dynamic Behaviour of the Domain
15
has two entry points, meaning it has two HTO-values associated with it, one for
each entry point.
D2U Indicates the positioning of trains going from down-to-up.
U2D Indicates the positioning of trains going from up-to-down.
As seen we have two values, called D2U and U2D, indicating where the train is
starting and where it goes. Throughout this report we will handle down (D)
as the left entry point of the Linear Section, while up (U) is the right entry
point. However, normally they would indicate the direction a train is moving in
relation to a reference point.
2.2.1.3
Point
The Points of the network, are responsible for diverting a train to the correct
path, such that it corresponds to a given route. The current state of the model-
checker only support single-points, i.e.
allow one track to be split into two,
or gather two existing tracks into one. An example of a point, can be seen in
Figure 2.6.
Linear
Minus
Plus
Stem
Figure 2.6: Representation of a point
As one can see from Figure 2.6, we have three end-points for a Point, in which
two can be considered stationary, however they may change position depending
of the position of Minus. The position of Plus and Stem is always in the linear-
section shown in Figure 2.6. However the Plus is also located in the same side
as Minus, while Stem is always in the opposite direction.
As a result of having three end-points, a Point has three HTO-values.
S2PM Indicates whether a train is going from the Stem towards one of the
branches Minus/Plus. Which branch that is used, depends on the posi-
tioning of the Point.
==PAGE:30==
16
Analysis
P2S Indicates the values for trains going from Plus-to-Stem.
M2S Indicates the values for trains going from Minus-to-Stem.
In order to control the positioning of a Point, several variables has been encoded
into the state of a Point, to simulate the behaviour of a Point.
Positioning
In relation to the positioning of a Point, i.e. in which direction
trains may move on a Point, we have two extra variables to determine in which
direction train should move.
POS
This describes the actually position of the Point, and it may have the
following values assigned.
• PLUS(0), means that it is positioned such that trains may move between
stem and plus.
• MINUS(1), means that it is positioned such that trains may move between
stem and minus.
• INTERMEDIATE(2), means that no trains may be located on the track,
as the Point is currently switching position. If a train is present on the
Point at this moment, it may result in a derailing of the train.
CMD
Even though the Point has an actual position, described by POS, it
is also commanded by the interlocking system to change its position.
This
is described by CMD, that can either be PLUS(0) or MINUS(1), the two valid
positions. This variable has been constructed to simulate, that there may be a
dierence between the actual positioning, and what has been ordered from the
interlocking system.
2.2.2
Additions
Additions or Extensions are all the features, that may be added to the Sec-
tions to extend the functionality of the Section that it is mounted on. In the
current state of the model-checker, we only account for Markerboards, however
Level-Crossings are in the process of being implemented.
==PAGE:31==
2.2 Dynamic Behaviour of the Domain
17
2.2.2.1
Markerboard
Represents the virtual signals that is to be installed on the track sections, which
will have a similar role as the physical signal in a relay-based interlocking system.
They decide, whether it is legal for a train to pass the given point, they are
installed at.
For this reason they have two values assigned, indicating the state of the marker-
board.
ACT
Represents the actual value of the Markerboard, and it may be OPEN(1)
or CLOSED(0). And a train may pass the Markerboard, if the value is OPEN(1).
CMD
Is the value that is commanded by the interlocking system, i.e. the
value that ACT should have.
This means that the values, CMD can take, are
identical to what ACT can be assigned.
There may be a dierence in values between ACT and CMD, due to delay in the
communication between the interlocking system and the trains.
2.2.2.2
Level Crossing
The Level Crossing is intended to simulate the places in which one may pass
the tracks sections, in order to get to the other side. These have not yet been
fully implemented into the model-checker, meaning we will not go into details
about the Level Crossing throughout the report.
2.2.3
Routes
As explained in Section 2.1.1, a specication, consist of an interlocking table,
that describes all routes that can be assigned within a given network.
Each route, will have a state that denes whether the route can be assigned,
already in use etc.
==PAGE:32==
18
Analysis
2.2.3.1
Route State
A Route has as any other element in the DSL a state, that describes whether it
is used or not. This state, called MODE, may have ve dierent values [3].
• FREE(0)  indicates that a route may be dispatched to a train. This is
the initial state of a route.
• MARKED(1)  is reached whenever a request for dispatching the route is
received. This makes the route ready to be allocated to a train.
• ALLOCATING(2)  is reached when the conguration of the required
elements are met, e.g. that all required Points are in the correct position.
• LOCKED(3)  whenever the allocations are completed within the AL-
LOCATING mode, the routes becomes locked preventing any other train
from entering the sections, that is used by the route.
• USED(4)  when in the USED mode, a route may release sections as the
train leaves (known as sequential releasing). This is new to the Danish
interlocking system. This enables, routes to be allocated faster, as con-
icting routes, no longer are using sections that is needed by a route. A
route enters the USED mode, when the train enter a section related to the
route.
In Figure 2.7 the relation between the states of a route can be seen.
The
transitions are as described, however a route stays in USED until all of its track
sections has been released sequentially, and it may be dispatched once more
when it reaches the FREE state.
LOCKED
USED
MARKED
FREE
dispatch(r)
allocate(r)
lock(r)
use(r)
release(r)
seqRelease(r)
ALLOCATING

The state of a route, has
an eect on the lockable elements, as it directly control which MODE a specic
section has. This eect on the life-cycle of a lockable element can be seen in
Figure 2.8.

Figure 2.8: Routes eect on a lockable element [3]
This shows how the dierent transitions in the life-cycle of a route, aects a
lockable element  in the gure the changed variable after a transition is shown
in red.
A lockable element starts in a free, non-vacant state with the previous (PREV)
element set to free as well. Whenever a route tries to allocate, it set a lockable
element to be EXLCK  which enables a train from entering the section, and it
will set the element to be non-vacant when the train enter the given section.
As a train enters an section, the route, states that is uses the given track section
 setting the lockable element to be in the USED state. Finally whenever the
previous section gets released, through sequential releasing, the PREV value is
ipped for the current section. After this, whenever the train leaves the section,
it will be set to vacant, allowing the route to release the current section. When
released, the next section in the route is notied, that it has been released
(indicating that it should ip its PREV-value)). This leaves the section in the
initial state, which allows other routes to use the section [3].
2.3
Similar Tools
As we are to model train networks, it would be interesting to see how others
have tried solve this issue. Train networks are a common eld of research, as
==PAGE:34==
20
Analysis
there are many safety regulation, one has to consider when creating tools to
work with said problem.
We will take a look at two existing tools, namely Safecap (a research project
from England) and a tool developed at DTU.
2.3.1
Safecap
Safecap is a tool, that was develop in order to improve capacity on train net-
works, while maintaining the required safety-level, by following the regulations
that is set up for train networks [5].
This sounds similar to what is expected of the tool-chain that RobustRailS
utilizes. However their respective domain are somewhat dierent, as there have
been taken some alternative decisions in Safecap opposed to RobustRailS. This
is mostly in regards, to how trains are located, as Safecap operates with a known
location and speed for a train, while RobustRailS determines an approximate
location, as it only knows how much of the train is present on a given track
section. However the tool oer some extendibility, as both the schema topology,
and which simulator to be used may be modied [6].
Figure 2.9 shows an example of a part of the front-end that Safecap consist of.
The way one constructs a network when using Safecap is simply by placing nodes,
that act as a form of connector between the segments that has a reference to
two nodes. This means that a segment can be somewhat related to the sections
of the RobustRailS project.
SafeCap is created with the help of Eclipse Modelling Framework (EMF) and
Graphical Modelling Framework (GMF). For this reason their approach has
to have the segments act as connections between the nodes, which is one of
the approaches, one may take when using GMF to design an editor.
More
information on GMF can be found in Section 3.2.2.
The fact that it is ultimately designed to create network layouts, depicting a
precise location of train makes it undesirable to work with in the current context,
as we only need an abstract representation of a trains current location.
==PAGE:35==
2.3 Similar Tools
21
Figure 2.9: Screenshot of the Safecap tool
2.3.2
Simulation of Relay Interlocking Systems
Another tool that also handles the creation of network, is a tool developed at
DTU, that is designed to handle relay interlocking system. However they have
a dierent approach at designing the physical network than SafeCap has.
As seen in Figure 2.10, we have a screenshot of the Simulation of Relay Inter-
locking Systems tool. The way one creates a network is in a block-fashion, in
which each section of the network is a block of its own. This means the designer
has no means of changing the look of a section after it has been placed. More-
over they have each section, xed on a specic line, which ensures that sections
are not placed at random locations, but instead is xed to a vertical position.
This allows for a clean look when designing a network.
The process of creating a network, is that one places the sections that needs to
be the network, and after a section has been placed, the ends may be connected
to other sections.
The fact that it is ultimately designed for relay interlocking systems, it consist of
many features that is not needed in the intended environment to be developed.
And it is likewise missing a lot of needed features. It is therefore not benecial
to extends this tool to t our needs, as it is also missing many of the features
that one would like from an editor (for our given domain).
==PAGE:36==
22
Analysis
Figure 2.10: Screenshot of the Simulation of Relay Interlocking Systems tool
2.4
Requirements
If we take a look at some of the functional and non-functional requirements,
to get an initial idea on how the environment should be constructed and what
features should be included.
2.4.1
Functional Requirements
The functional requirement are all the requirements that one expects to be
included in the environment  meaning the features that has to be included
in order for the environment to be a success. The requirements that has been
found is listed below.
Create Specication The most basic functional requirement, is that we can
actually create a specication that correspond to the DSL described in Sec-
tion 2.1.1. This means support for both network layouts and interlocking
tables.
Graphical Editor The environment must have a graphical editor, that sup-
port the graphical domain specic language described in Chapter 4.
Verify Static Well-formedness It should support static-verication of the
==PAGE:37==
2.4 Requirements
23
constructed specication  such that incorrect specication can be cor-
rected. This may be done with the existing static checker that is already
implemented in the tool-chain, as explained in Section 2.1.2.
Generate Models and Safety Properties The environment should be able
to generate the model and safety properties, and verify the correctness of
the specication towards these. For this purpose the existing tool-chain
can be utilized.
Visualization of Counter-Examples The environment should support an
easy way to walk through a generated counter-example. This needs to
correspond with what will be dened for the graphical domain specic
language.
Overview of Safety Properties An overview of the result for each safety
property must be provided. This should show whether the safety property
succeeded or failed.
Reset State It should be possible to reset a current interlocking system to its
initial state  such that no trains or routes are active (and points are in a
neutral position).
Extendibility The environment should support some extendibility, such that
there may be added new features after deployment.
New Components It should be possible to add a new component that
adds extra content to the existing environment. This will allow for
future development  as one can add extra functionality without mod-
ifying the existing environment.
New Elements The environment should likewise support for easy im-
plementation of track-side elements  as these may be added to the
model-checker at a later time. This will provide the environment,
with a great level of exibility, as one will not need to modify the
environment to add support for a new element.
XML Support There should be integrated XML support, for utilizing the
existing tool-chain. Since the tool-chain accepts the existing DSL in an
XML format, this is a crucial requirement for the environment.
Labels Information within the core of a network, such as IDs for elements,
length of sections etc. should be able to be displayed as labels on the
network-layout. The labels will also need to support editing.
Print Support The created network layout, must be possible to print, such
that one can analyse the network by hand  this also includes the current
state of the interlocking system (shown graphical)
==PAGE:38==
24
Analysis
2.4.2
Non-functional Requirements
Even though, we do not have that many non-functional requirement, they are
still important for the development, as they have some eect on the design
decisions to be made.
Single Environment It is desirable if the environment is contained within a
single executable, such that the user has all actions available in one window
 this will greatly increase the usability of the tool, as one will not need
to switch between dierent windows.
Linux As the existing tool-chain, only can be executed on Linux, it should
be desirable to have the environment run on Linux as well. Otherwise
one would have to make a network-based environment to communicate
between the platforms.
The most important non-functional requirement, is that we will need it to be
supported on Linux, as it will be infeasible to make a network-based tool. If
Linux is not supported, one would have to have two operating systems running
at once and switch between these constantly. All in all it would be undesirable
from an end-users perspective.
==PAGE:39==
Chapter 3
Domain Specic Languages
As the graphical domain specic language is to be designed on top an already
existing domain specic language (DSL), a small description of what an DSL
is, and what advantages and disadvantages it gives you as a developer will be
given.
After this, a small examination of frameworks, that may be utilized in the de-
velopment of our graphical domain specic language will be conducted. The
examined frameworks will then be compared, such that we will utilize the most
suitable framework in the development of the graphical domain specic lan-
guage.
3.1
Domain Specic Language
A domain specic language (DSL) is a language that has been designed to de-
scribe a particular domain, such that is can model situations inside the given
domain exceptionally well. An example of a domain would be interlocking sys-
tems for railway networks  which is what we deal with.
The purpose of a DSL is to increase the abstraction level of a language such
==PAGE:40==
26
Domain Specic Languages
that it deal exceptionally well with a given domain. One needs to nd the level-
of-abstraction one wants to achieve when designing a DSL compared to using a
general purpose language, such as JAVA [7].
A well-designed DSL will oer an increase in productivity with anyone involved
in the process of using the domain in question. This is as it oers capabilities
to easily construct solutions with objects related to the domain [8]
However using a DSL is not all benecial, as there are both advantages and
disadvantage when using a DSL instead of a general-purpose language. In Sec-
tion 3.1.1, several advantages is listed, while we in Section 3.1.2 list some of the
disadvantages of using a DSL.
3.1.1
Advantages
Using a DSL has several advantages, when dealing with the domain it is designed
for.
Incorporated Domain Knowledge The most obvious advantage of using a
DSL, is the incorporated knowledge of the domain. This ensures that one
cannot establish anything that is syntactically wrong  as well-known facts
of the particular domain are built into the language [7].
Easier error detection Due to the lower expressiveness of a DSL, it is usually
easier to avoid mistakes when using the DSL. And in case an error is made,
it is easier to spot what the error is and how to x it [8].
Improved Productivity Due to the increased level of abstraction, a DSL will
most likely increase the productivity. This is also one of the main reason
why one would utilize a DSL instead of a general purpose language [7] [8].
Communication with Domain Experts As the DSL incorporates knowl-
edge about the domain, it is easier for the programmer and domain experts
to communicate with each other about issues. The domain experts has an
easier job of guring out, what should be done in order to achieve what he
intend, while the programmer will get knowledge about the domain while
working with the DSL [8].
All in all using a DSL is benecial when using the language, as it helps everyone
involved getting a better understanding of a solution within the domain.
==PAGE:41==
3.2 Developing Framework
27
3.1.2
Disadvantages
However a DSL is not all advantages, as there are some disadvantages.
Specialized tools One issue with DSLs in general is that they may need spe-
cialized tools in order to work. This means that people need to be trained
in using the language and tools revolved around the language. And the
tools will have to be maintained in case changes to the language are made.
All in all, it may be a costly aair to use a DSL or even design a DSL for
a given domain, instead of using existing languages [7] [8].
Language Cacophony Another issue that may arise when using DSLs, is that
people may be overwhelmed of learning yet another language. This may
cause issues in the development phase, especially in case a multitude of
languages are used in the development [8].
However these disadvantages are not critical unless one has a shortage of mon-
ey/time or are already using a lot of other DSLs. In our case however we have a
single DSL that needs to be extended, which means that it certainly is benecial
to use a DSL.
3.2
Developing Framework
When developing a domain specic language (DSL), it is infeasible to start from
scratch and develop a complete framework around the given DSL. One would
instead utilize some of the excellent frameworks, that is already present on the
market.
By using an existing framework, one can lower ones initial workload of creating
the tool that implements the DSL. It will likewise also decrease support eort, as
more people may know of the framework used for the development. There exist
several frameworks that may be used for this purpose, in which two of these will
be examine to determine which is the better option to use in our situation.
The two frameworks, that we will take a closer look at is:
MetaEdit+ Tool designed to create DSLs, with little eort, as one can easily
create the dierent aspects through an easy to use graphical user interface.
One can even create a modelling language without a single line of code. [9]
==PAGE:42==
28
Domain Specic Languages
Graphical Modelling Framework (GMF) Incorporated into Eclipse as a
plug-in, it oers a tool that enables the developer to easily and quickly
create a graphical editor, utilizing Eclipse Modelling Framework (EMF)
and Graphical Editing Framewor (GEF) as its core. It was created due to
frustration on how dicult GEF was to work with. [10]
So on one hand we have a tool made specic for creating DSLs, while on the
other hand, have a more general tool, which may oers options one would not
have with a more specialized tool, as MetaEdit+.
3.2.1
MetaEdit+
Released in 1995, MetaEdit+, has a long history behind it, and can be considered
a stable and extensive program.
The rst research related to the tool, was
performed in the late 80's and early 90's, known then by the name MetaEdit,
later commercialized as MetaEdit+ in 1995. [11].
3.2.1.1
Data Model
The basic requirement, when developing a DSL is the language denition itself.
MetaEdit+ utilizes Graph-Object-Property-Port-Role-Relationship (GOPPRR)
for this purpose, and as the name suggest it is used to dene relations and roles
of each component that is available in the DSL [12]. The dierent elements,
that one can utilize when using GOPPRR is:
Graph The model containing the other objects  this is often the diagram of
the solution.
Object The main elements of the graph  typical has alot of relationships with
other objects. An object could for instance be a Markerboard, which could
have a relationship with a Section.
Property Describing an attribute of an object, and will most likely be shown
as a simple label on the graph.
Port Describes an area in which a role may connect to. Its purpose is to limit
in which locations roles can be connected, instead of being placed at an
arbitrary location.
Role Gives an object a role for a relationship  this could be that a Markerboard
must be active for a train to pass it.
==PAGE:43==
3.2 Developing Framework
29
Relationship Connects the objects together, such that they know of each
other.
With this data-model, one will be able to dene a DSL, in which one can easily
set up the relations between the dierent entities, that one will have in the DSL.
In order to dene the language, there are several tools that helps the user dene
the entities contained in the DSL.
3.2.1.2
Graphical Appearance
A strong feature of MetaEdit+, is the possibility to dene how each graphical
entity must be shown in the editor. This can either be done with an built-in
editor, or one can simply import image les from the le-system.
This feature means that one with ease can re-dene, how a given element looks
in the editor. An example of what can be constructed with this feature, can be
seen in Figure 3.1, in which a train-network has been dened.
This is similar to what could be expected of the graphical domain specic lan-
guage to be developed. And it shows the capability of using MetaEdit+ for
dening a DSL  as it can provide a graphical denition out the box.
3.2.1.3
Constraints
Setting up validation of a network or graph is easy when using MetaEdit+, as
one can easily dene what objects may be contained in a relationship, and how
many entities may be part of said relationship.
Moreover it is possible to easily dene on graph level, several constraints, that
the entities within the graph must comply with.
This could e.g.
be that a
property must be unique for all objects of the same type (which could be the
IDs).
By having these constraints, one can easily ensure that no violations are estab-
lished when constructing a network (according to the static properties).
1http://www.metacase.com/images/TCD_d1.png
==PAGE:44==
30
Domain Specic Languages
Figure 3.1: Example of a Train Network in Metaedit+.1
3.2.1.4
Generators
MetaEdit+, has support for generating various code or documentation, with the
use of MetaEdit+ Reporting Language (MERL). This could be used to write
a given graph into an XML le, which will make us able to run the existing
tool-chain to validate the interlocking system.
The generators simply crawls through the models, and outputs a le according
to a template used by the generator, that determines how to interpret a given
object.
3.2.1.5
Simulation
MetaEdit+ in it self does not support any form of simulation, however it has an
API, that can be utilized to communicate with other tools, that may be part of
a tool-chain.
==PAGE:45==
3.2 Developing Framework
31
This means that one could have an external program that calls MetaEdit+ to
retrieve the XML for a given graph  containing the network. This XML le will
then be used by the existing tool, to verify the interlocking system and provide
the related results. One outstanding issue that MetaEdit+ suers from, is that
one cannot alter the appearance of the network in an easy way. This would be
required in order to support visualization of the counter-examples generated.
Even though it is not directly support, one could create a visualization, by
having dynamic symbols that is changed when a property value are changed [13].
However a better solution would be to make a separation of concern, such that
we will utilize MetaEdit+ to make the domain, while another tool will be used
to visualize the results.
This approach will create an extra workload, as an
visualizer will have to be created to represent the dierent states  which will
needs to look exactly like MetaEdit+, ultimately giving two denitions of the
same appearance.
3.2.2
Graphical Modelling Framework
The Graphical Modelling Framework (GMF) is a framework that is part of a
larger modelling project for Eclipse, namely the Eclipse Modelling Project. In
this project, GMF, act as a framework for dening the graphical denitions of
a domain model.
However for the Eclipse Modelling Project, we have the Eclipse Modelling
Framework (EMF) at its core, which is the foundation of any other project
which occurs in the Eclipse Modelling Project [14].
GMF utilizes EMF, by having EMF dene the core features that GMF needs
for creating an editor, such as undo/redo functionality, notifying about model
changes etc. EMF is likewise used to dene the domain-model that will be used
as a basis for the editor to be generated in GMF.
GMF is still a young framework compared to other frameworks, and is still
under development, however as it is open-source, one could alter any source
code, to x a bug in case one appears. As it is a young framework, there may
occur issues when one uses GMF, and this is very likely a result of it still being
developed.
==PAGE:46==
32
Domain Specic Languages
3.2.2.1
Eclipse Modelling Framework
EMF is as the name suggest a modelling framework, that utilize the feature that
is present in Eclipse, to help the user create models using UML as the notation.
The beauty about EMF is that it from the created UML model can generate
Java code according to the model dened [15]. This is an excellent feature, as it
decreases development cost drastically, as the structure of a program is set up
for you according to the dened model.
When modelling with EMF, one creates an ecore model, which is a meta-
metamodel [15], as it can describe itself. An ecore model may contain four
dierent types of elements:
EClass is used to dene a class, and is what will be generated into Java classes.
It may also generate interface, in case this is dened.
EAttribute is single attribute for an instance of an EClass.
EReference is used to connect the dierent EClass instances together in re-
lations, with a cardinality.
EDataType is a representation of a simple type or object, such that the con-
tained data may be used as the type for an EAttribute.
An example of an ECore model can be seen in Figure 3.2, which depicts an
example of how the existing DSL for interlocking systems may be established
with EMF.
Figure 3.2: Interlocking System modelled in EMF
==PAGE:47==
3.2 Developing Framework
33
As seen we have several EClass elements, i.e. Network, Markerboard etc. which
describes the core features of the model. These elements are then connected
through EReference in various ways and contain several EAttributes. The
only type not represented is a EDateType, which could contain a Linear Section,
enabling it to be used as an attribute.
Do note that in this case an instance of a network, would only be able to save
if all instantiated elements are in a contained path from the top element, that
in this case is the Network. A contained references indicate that the element
which the reference originates from owns the referenced element.
Features
When one nally has a nalized model and decides to generate his
code, a well-designed framework may be utilized. It contains many of the fea-
tures that one needs when creating a program, and these features can be utilize
when one customizes the code.
Notication and Adapters
Every EObject, i.e.
instance of EClass is a
notier, which enables it to notify everyone who listens to it for changes. This
res every time, the object changes a reference or attributes, meaning external
objects may keep an eye on the state of a given object.
Any object that wishes to get notied when a specic object changes, will have
to utilize an adapter  this will catch any notication from any object that it
is assigned to. An adapter will have to subscribe to any objects, that it wishes
to get notications from. And it is not limited to listen to a single object, but
may be deployed to several objects [15].
This is a nice feature, as it allows a developer to easily make his objects get
notications from a particular object, thereby maintaining a consistent state
throughout the program.
Command Framework
One interesting feature of EMF, is the support for
commands, which creates an undo/redo functionality, as changes to the model
are recorded as they happen, enabling one to undo what happened doing the
model-change. These commands may change any object in various ways, such
as setting a value or updating a reference, allowing for complex command to be
executed. Several commands may be combined into a single command, to be
executed in one step.
When executing a command, one will have to fetch the command-stack of the
==PAGE:48==
34
Domain Specic Languages
current editor, and the command will then be appended and execute upon the
provided command-stack. This will alter the model-instance, as stated within
the provided command. Finally this executed command may then be undone
and redone as one pleases, giving back the original state of the editor [15].
All changes to a model, must therefore be executed within a command, such
that one can undo/redo the changes that would happen.
The given features are just a small sub-set of what EMF supports, however they
are crucial for using EMF as the foundation of a program.
3.2.2.2
Graphical Modelling Framework
GMF is what is used to dene the graphical front-end for the domain specied in
EMF  which is done by dening which shape each object of the domain should
have. One can also decide not to have a graphical representation for an object
 in case it should only appear in the model. However if one decides to provide
an object with a graphical denition, there are several standard representation
to choose from, such as rectangles or circles.
When code is generated from the specied visual denition, an editor will be
ready to be used out of the box  in case one has written some Object Constrain
Language (OCL), that can be used as rules (if this is the case, it will contain
rules for relations etc.). However the graphical denition is used within a viewer
 where EditParts may be placed. An EditPart is a graphical denition of
an EObject. The graphical appearance of an EditPart may be altered after
code generation, such that one gets the graphical denition that is intended, if
it could not be dened before the code was generated.
As GMF is built on-top of Graphical Editing Framework (GEF)  it borrows
many features from GEF.
Tools and Palette
When using GMF, and one wishes to initialize an instance
of an EditPart, one utilizes a palette which consists of several tools.
The tools has dierent tasks, some are there for the user to create EditParts,
while others are just helping the user, move existing EditParts around in the
diagram [14].
In many cases, one cannot utilize the standard tools, as they do not correspond
to the work-ow that is intended for the editor.
==PAGE:49==
3.2 Developing Framework
35
Boundary EditParts
One feature that is interesting, is that one can create
EditParts that is stuck to the edge of another EditPart's boundaries. This
can be used to visualize a relation between the two EditParts, and it results in
the EditPart being placed on the edge, and it is unable to move away from the
other EditPart, i.e. it is stuck to the boundaries of the other EditPart, and
will move wherever the parent EditPart moves.
Nodes and Connections
The only means of specifying graphical appear-
ance, is by using either nodes or connections. Nodes may take many shapes,
however they will always have a rectangle dened as their area, which describes
the boundaries in which the EditPart may be selected adn drawn within. For
the connection, it is as the name suggest, a connection that is used to connect
the dierent nodes in a diagram together to form a larger network.
One can dene several rules, that a given connection must follow, such as not
connecting to nodes of the same type. The visual appearance of a connection is
a simple line, however it may be modied to gain dierent eects, e.g. having a
dashed eect.
3.2.3
Evaluation
The two described frameworks, MetaEdit+ and GMF, both have advantages and
disadvantages, in case one would utilize it for the environment to be constructed.
The only reason not to choose MetaEdit+ over GMF, is the fact that is has
no native support for simulating the results, that will be given as counter-
examples from the existing tool-chains. This is a crucial point, as this is one of
the requirements for the environment. In case one would utilize MetaEdit+, a
separate tool for visualizing the results would have to be specied, which means
that one would have to perform extra work  and potentially create the separate
tool in a framework such as GMF. Based on this requirement alone, we choose
not to look further into what MetaEdit+ capabilities are, and instead went with
developing the solution in GMF.
Even though the development time in GMF is much larger than in MetaEdit+,
it gives us more freedom on how the given environment should behave. This is
as it is based on Java and running in Eclipse, meaning we can collect all the
dierent tools/views to be created under a single environment, which makes
it more usable  and ts well with the non-functional requirement, that our
environment should be supported within a single environment.
==PAGE:50==
36
Domain Specic Languages
If MetaEdit+, would have supported the freedom that GMF provides, it would
have been the perfect choice for the environment, as dening a DSL is easy and
very straight-forward.
==PAGE:51==
Chapter 4
Visual Representation
Given the DSL described in Chapter 2, we will need to describe the graphical
denition for the DSL, such that we get a graphical domain-specic language.
Currently the DSL contains three implemented elements, namely Linear Sec-
tions, Point Sections and Markerboards.
We will not focus on dening the
graphical denition for Level Crossings, as these have not yet been fully imple-
mented by the model checker.
4.1
Graphical Appearance
The network in our graphical domain specic language will be represented as
a canvas in which one may place the sections and markerboards. This will give
each entity in the network a visual location (which will not be related to their
physical location).
The graphical language will not relate any visual location to its physical location,
as we will abstract away from the actual dimension of entities, that is to be
contained in the network.
However, as the network is to be represented by a canvas, it will not have a
==PAGE:52==
38
Visual Representation
visual appearance other than being a white area, in which other entities may be
placed. This means that a network will be a container, that is to contain the
entities within the provided DSL.
4.1.1
Linear Section
As stated in Section 2.1.1.1, a linear section represent the stretches of the net-
work that may contain the markerboards (that holds the virtual signals for
controlling the trains).
The stretches that is to be represented by the linear sections, may in reality
contain curves, such that trains can divert their travelling direction slightly.
However in the current DSL, curves are not modelled, and we have chosen to
abstract away from having curves on any linear section in a network.
With this abstraction, a graphical denition of a linear section is straight for-
ward, since we can represent it as a simple line, as seen in Figure 4.1.
Figure 4.1: Visual representation of Linear Track Section
The reason, we have abstracted away from curves on linear sections, is that
this could easily be modelled by having a speed limit on each track section of a
network. By having a speed limit one could simulate curves behind the scene,
and it would be possible to detect whether a train will derail on a section (if it
was going to fast). In the current state, this is not needed as we operate with
an abstract location of trains, in which we only know where the head and tail
of the train is located.
4.1.1.1
Visible Information
A linear section has multiple pieces of information that one would like to be
able to visually see, when it is placed within a network. As described in Sec-
tion 2.1.1.1, a linear section contains two pieces of information that one could
nd interesting, i.e. the ID and length of the section.
This information will be placed as labels, indicating the value of the given
parameter. The location of these labels, will be given as shown in Figure 4.2, in
which the ID is to be placed on-top of section, while the length will be located
underneath the section.
==PAGE:53==
4.1 Graphical Appearance
39
t10
t11
1000
575
Figure 4.2: Visual information available for Linear Track Sections
It was chosen to have the length of the track represented as a label and not as a
visual state for the track section  meaning the width of the track section would
represent the length. This was as it would make the diagram for the network
unusable, as it would easily get too wide to work with. For this reason, it was
chosen to have the length contained within a label, resulting in a more condense
network-layout.
Regarding the UP and DOWN direction assigned to a track section, it was
chosen to have the DOWN direction be placed at the left-end of any linear
track section. This means that we at all times, will have that an end in the UP
direction, always meets an end in the DOWN direction  ensuring that no track
sections are connected in a wrong manor.
About textual information
It should be noted that the textual information
such as ID or length for the linear section, should not be shown at all times.
The user should have the option to either have it shown or not  making the
graphical domain specic language somewhat vague on what information will
be shown.
This also applies to any other entity within the domain  meaning any textual
information (to be placed in a label) is optional. Moreover it will be possible to
change the value, contained in the label, by directly altering the provided text.
4.1.1.2
Markerboards
One signicant part of a linear track section, is the possibility to have multiple
markerboards attached  with a virtual signal embedded into them. The linear
track section, may have up to two markerboards associated with it  one in each
end of the track section.
The graphical appearance of a markerboard, can be seen in Figure 4.3, and it
will be represented by having an arrow point towards the track section, that
it has been assigned to. Depending of the travelling direction, the orientation
of the arrow changes, such that it is always located to the right of the track
(from ones perspective), having the arrow point onto the track section, that it
==PAGE:54==
40
Visual Representation
is attached to. The means that we have two dierent markerboards, as seen in
Figure 4.3.
The UP markerboard, shown in Figure 4.3a, can clearly be seen as pointing
inward to the track, that the train is currently on when travelling upward  as
the train is moving from left to right. And the same can be said for the DOWN
markerboard, seen in Figure 4.3b, just in the opposite direction.
(a) UP markerboard
(b) DOWN markerboard
Figure 4.3: Graphical appearance of markerboards
As stated in Section 2.1.1.1, a markerboard has information that should be
visible for the end-user, i.e. an ID and its distance from the end of the track
section that the markerboard is attached to.
This information will be located such that the distance will be visible near the
point, in which the markerboard is attached to the given section (this position
of the point is not representative of the actually distance to the end). While the
ID will be placed either on-top (if DOWN) or underneath (if UP) the markerboard.
This is if the user decides to have them visible.

linear section that a given markerboard is installed at, since it is directly

4.1.2
Point Section
As stated in Section 2.2, a point section is what enables a train to divert onto
a dierent lane  such that is may utilize another area of the network. For this
reason it has as described in Section 2.1.1.1, up to three neighbouring sections.
And two of these neighbour are located in the same end of the track  meaning a
single section gets split into two, or two sections are gathered to one, depending
on the travel direction.
For this reason, one can model a point section, as a line that gets split into
two, representing the switch that is present on the point section. The suggested
graphical appearance of a point, can be seen in Figure 4.5, which shows one of
the four congurations, a point section may take.
Figure 4.5: Visual representation of Point
As stated, a point section, may have four dierent congurations, describing in
which direction the tracks should get split. In all congurations, it is the MINUS
location of the point that may change its position, i.e. the sub-section of the
point diverting away from the base of the point (the base is the linear section
of the point). Given the conguration, seen in Figure 4.5, we are missing the
remaining three congurations, which can be seen in Figure 4.6.
Figure 4.6: Dierent congurations of Points
==PAGE:56==
42
Visual Representation
By having these four congurations of a point, one can model any given situation
regarding a single point.
4.1.2.1
Visual Information
As for the linear sections, we have similar information that needs to be visualised
for the point sections of the network. This include an ID and the length of the
section.

Figure 4.7: Visual information visible on a Point
In Figure 4.7, a point is shown with the information. The ID is placed to the left,
while the length of the point section is placed to the right. The information will
always be located next to the straight section of the point, either underneath
or on top of the section. The gives a clear understanding on what point section
the given information belongs to.
Regarding what each end of the point section corresponds to, i.e. STEM, PLUS
and MINUS, one can easily spot it by looking at the conguration of the point
section. This is as it is always the lone end, that is the STEM, which in the case
of Figure 4.7 would be the left-most end.
The PLUS and MINUS are therefore the other ends, where the MINUS is always
the one diverting away from the straight section of the point. In case of the
situation in Figure 4.7, the MINUS end is therefore the top-most end. In case the
MINUS end is moved according to the congurations shown in Figure 4.6, the
other ends are updated accordingly, such that the STEM is always the lone end.
