each element will be provided, stating how each element will look when the
internal state is changed for the element.
==PAGE:58==
44
Visual Representation
4.2
State Visualization
At any given time, the train network may have a state indicating where trains
are located, how points are set etc. The graphical appearance for such states,
will describe the dynamic behaviour of the network, and should therefore be
easy for the user to dierentiate between the dierent states. This should be
true for both the whole network, as well as for each individual object within
the network. A state for an object, could be in what position a point section is
currently positioned in.
4.2.1
State of Points
As mentioned in Section 2.2, a point section has a variable, POS, that states in
which position the point section is currently located at.
The POS indicates the position of the point section, and ultimately in which
direction a train may move, when using the point section. Since POS may take
three dierent values, we need three graphical denitions of a point section 
each representing a dierent state.
One can see the dierent positions, as having assigned an active route through
a point section. And this may be visualized by having the active route through
the point section, have the same colour as the rest of the network, namely black.
While the inactive route will be slightly dimmed, getting a light-grey colour
indicating it is not used. With this approach the stretch from the STEM to the
switch in the middle will always be coloured black.
Figure 4.9 shows the three dierent positions a point section may take. The
intermediate state is only shown when active, and as one can see it will appear
between the two sections of the point (representing that the point is in the
process of switching).
(a) Plus direction
(b) Minus direction
(c) Intermediate
Figure 4.9: Visual representations of a Point
==PAGE:59==
4.2 State Visualization
45
By having the point sections this way, one can easily see which position a specic
point section has, at any given point in time  and easily spot potential errors
that has occurred.
4.2.2
State of Markerboards
The state of a markerboard is as stated in Section 2.2, described by the variable
ACT, that represent the active state of the virtual signal attached to the marker-
board. The ACT, is therefore what will be utilized to visualize the state of an
markerboard  as it is what trains would see, when passing the markerboard.
The ACT attribute, may take two values, either it is OPEN or CLOSED. As we
have the visualization of markerboards represented as an arrow, one can have
the arrow change colour depending on the value of ACT. For this reason, we
have that when the markerboard is in the OPEN state, the arrow will be green
indicating a train may pass. And when in the CLOSED state, it will instead be
indicated by the arrow being red.
The two dierent types of markerboards can be seen in Figure 4.10, and as one
can see it will give a clear representation of what state the markerboard has.
(a) OPEN signal
(b) CLOSED signal
Figure 4.10: Visual representation of markerboards
By having this simple representation for the state of a markerboard, we ensure
that one cannot mistake a given state. This is as only a single colour is shown
when a state is active  enabling the user to easily dierentiate between the
states, and not mistake a CLOSED signal for an OPEN signal.
4.2.2.1
Alternative approach
Another approach for visualizing the state of the markerboards could be to have
both a green and red dot at the edge of the gure. As seen in Figure 4.11 one
would have that, when a state is active the colour representing the state, would
==PAGE:60==
46
Visual Representation
be a brighter colour indicating  meaning one can identify the active state of
the markerboard, by looking at which colour is highlighted with a bright colour.
(a) OPEN signal
(b) CLOSED signal
Figure 4.11: Alternative representation of markerboards
The reason, this representation would not be the best approach, is that a user
may mistake a CLOSED signal for an OPEN signal and vice versa. This is
avoided in the other approach, as only a single colour is shown at any given
time.
One positive feature about this approach, is that all markerboards has the same
visual appeal, as the arrow has a consistent colour throughout the whole net-
work. However due to the increased chances of getting mistakes, when reading
the network-layout, this approach was not chosen.
4.2.3
Positioning of Trains
As stated in Section 2.2, the domain operates with an abstraction on where
trains are located, as each end of section will have an HTO-value assigned. These
values will have to be visualized, such that one can identify where trains are
located, and in which direction they are travelling, according to the value of the
HTO-attribute.
The graphical appearance of the train-location, must not only be able to rep-
resent the location of the train, but also when errors happen, e.g. if a head-on
collision occurs between two trains (happens when two trains are located on the
same section, travelling in opposite directions).
With the use of the HTO-attributes, we can consider a train to have three parts
that may be located on any track section. These parts are as stated by the
HTO-value, the head, the tail or a middle section of the train. Each part must be
shown when the corresponding bit in HTO is set, where the middle of the train
section is shown in the situation, where neither the head nor tail of a train is
present, but a section is occupied (O).
==PAGE:61==
4.2 State Visualization
47
We will now propose four dierent approaches on how this may be visualized,
each having both advantages and disadvantages. In the end we will have a small
discussion about which approach is most suitable for the given problem.
4.2.3.1
Train next to Track Section
One approach that could be utilized is to have the trains located next to the
track section that they currently occupies. And in order to vary the look when
a train is in the three dierent states, we have the three visual representations
shown in Figure 4.12.
(a) Head of Train
(b) Tail of Train
(c) Whole Train
Figure 4.12: Looks of Train Placement
As seen in Figure 4.12, we have three looks for a train when they are placed
next to a track section.
These representations, may be combined in various
ways, such that one can illustrate the dierent values HTO can get. This is e.g.
the case when both the head and tail is present on the same track section. In
case these were on the same track section, we get the representation shown in
Figure 4.13.
Figure 4.13: Head and Tail on same Track Section
This gives us the opportunity to show every state, that a train may have on
a track section. The reason for having the train located on top of the track,
as shown in Figure 4.12 and Figure 4.13, is to illustrate in which direction the
given train is moving. So if the train is placed on-top of the section, it will be
moving from UP to DOWN, while trains placed under the section will be travelling
from DOWN to UP. Even though it may look like multiple trains may be placed
on a given section, due to having the travelling directions separated, the section
will however still only be able to contain a single train (as we otherwise would
get errors).
A problem that would arise, if all trains were to be placed on-top of the section,
is that it would disrupt the look of the contained gures. This is as they would
be placed on-top of each other, mixing the given gures together. While having
==PAGE:62==
48
Visual Representation
them placed both on-top and underneath the section, enables one to easily
detect, which trains are travelling in a given direction. An example of having the
gure placed both on-top and underneath the section, can be seen in Figure 4.14.
Figure 4.14: Example of two train on the same track section travelling in
opposite directions
The case shown in Figure 4.14 represents a state that would be unsafe, since
the two trains would make a head-on collision with each other. Even though it
may seem like they are able to pass each other, this is not the case, as the track
section represent a singular track, in which only one train may travel on, at a
any given time.
This way of representing the trains, gives a clear representation on where trains
are located on the network  and in which direction they are moving. Further-
more, one can with ease see, what sections a specic train is occupying, as a
train will be represented by a consecutive gure that spans that occupied track
sections. An example of a train spanning multiple track sections, can be seen
in Figure 4.15.
Figure 4.15: Train located on multiple track sections
And one can easily see that Figure 4.15, represents a single train, occupying
three track sections, moving from UP to DOWN. A train will only consist of one
head and tail, however may consists of multiple middle train sections  in case
the train spans several track sections.
4.2.3.2
Train on Track Section
In order to represent the trains, one could use a slight modication of the pro-
posed solution, given in Section 4.2.3.1. However opposed to that solution, one
would simply place the train representation directly on the track sections, that
they occupy. So in case we would have the head of the train present on a track
section, it would look as shown in Figure 4.16.
==PAGE:63==
4.2 State Visualization
49
Figure 4.16: Head of train placed on track section
As seen it is clear in which direction the train is moving, as it is indicated by the
pointy end of the train representation. To symbolise the full length of a train
the gure would extend over several track section, as it was done in Figure 4.15.
However in this case, we would have the train lowered, such that it is placed on
both sides of the track sections.
There is however a slight problem when utilizing this method to symbolise trains,
and that is the fact, that it will look slightly confusing when an error occurs in
the system. An example for this is shown in Figure 4.17, in which two trains
are in a head-on collision. In one end, we have a complete train (both head
and tail included) that is moving from DOWN to UP, while the head of another
train has entered the track section, moving from UP to DOWN. As one can see, the
representation is slightly confusing, as the gure are mixed together, meaning
it is hard to decode what is happening, especially for a person not used to the
domain.
Figure 4.17: Collision when trains are located on tracks
4.2.3.3
Figures at end of Track Section
As the HTO-value represents what parts of the train is present when moving
from an end of the section, a simple solution would be to display the state at
the given end. This means that a small gure will represent, which parts of the
train is currently going from an end to any of the other ends of the section.
Each dierent segment of a train, would then be represented by the gures
shown in Figure 4.18, and they may be combined in various way. This would
allow both the head and tail of the train to be shown at the same time for a
given entry point of a section (in case the full train is contained in the section).
To summarize the meaning of the dierent symbols shown in Figure 4.18, we
have:
==PAGE:64==
50
Visual Representation
(a) Head of Train
(b) Tail of Train
(c) Whole Train
Figure 4.18: Symbols indicating state of a train on a track section
Head of Train This indicates that the front-section of the train has entered
the track section, whereas the tail may be located on a dierent track sec-
tion. A reversed symbol should be available, such that the travel direction
of the train is clear.
Tail of Train Indicates that the end of the train is present on the track section.
Whole Train Indicate that neither the head nor tail is present on the track
section. It simply means that the train is too long to t onto the track
section, meaning it is used to inform that there is a train on the section.
The only two symbols, that may be combined are the head and tail of the
train, as it symbolises that a given train is completely contained within a track
section. The symbols representing a whole train is used to state that the section
is occupied but neither the head nor tail of a train is present. Or in case this
happens, an error should be thrown, as we have reached an illegal state.
When the symbols of Figure 4.18, are visualized next to a track section, they
will be placed on the end of the track section, that the train entered. This would
be as shown in Figure 4.19.
Figure 4.19: Positioning of Train Symbols
In the case of Figure 4.19, we have that the head of a train is entering from the
STEM of the point going towards either PLUS or MINUS. In the other end,
we have that a train is contained completely in the point heading from MINUS
to the STEM. The given scenario, however would be a case of an error in the
network, as there would be a head-on collision between the two trains.
==PAGE:65==
4.2 State Visualization
51
This method gives a clear representation of where trains has entered and in
which direction they are moving. However it has the fault that the symbols
are small  and one can with ease miss that two trains are actually colliding
with each other, if one overlooks a symbol. It will therefore be easy to get lost
when determining what an error might have been caused by, especially in case
we have a visually long linear section (in which the opposing symbols, cannot
t on a single screen).
4.2.3.4
Arrows near Track Section
A simple method to represent the location of trains, would be to have them
symbolised by arrows, indicating in which direction they are moving. The arrows
would then be placed corresponding to how much of the train is present on a
given section. In case of only having the head or tail of the train present, only
half of the section would have arrows present.
To further increase the chances of detecting where trains are located, the given
section could be coloured green or any other colour to represent that a train is
present on the section.
An example of this approach can be seen in Figure 4.20, in which there are a
train moving form left-to-right, and one moving from right-to-left, each diverting
onto dierent lanes according to the other train.
Figure 4.20: Simplied visualisation of Trains positioning
However Figure 4.20, also visualizes an issue that this method has, as one cannot
see how many trains that are moving around the network.
In the example
given, one could potentially have two trains going from right-to-left, but if they
are following each other, one would not be able to detect this. Moreover if a
train would drive into the rear of another train, one would have a dicult time
spotting why the system has reported an error, as one can only see a single train
on the stretch.
In case one monitors the movement at all times, the solution might be a possi-
bility, as one can monitor that a train is located behind another, since they will
==PAGE:66==
52
Visual Representation
release segments before the next train enters it - or if the train really does oc-
cupy all the stated segments, as the tail of train will be present before releasing
a segment.
4.2.3.5
Discussion
When looking at the proposed methods for visualizing the state of trains, we
have a clear favourite, namely the method of displaying the trains next to the
track, see Section 4.2.3.1. This is as it gave a clear and unambiguous view on
the current state.
Unlike the other methods it had no particular disadvantages, as it provides a
clear representation on how much of a train is located of a section, and in which
direction it is are moving. It is therefore most appropriate to have the trains
represented next to the tracks  as it provides an user-friendly approach for
decoding the state of each section.
The best alternative to having the train next to the track, would be to have the
small gures at the end of the track sections, as discussed in Section 4.2.3.3.
However this has the disadvantages, that the gure may become too small com-
pared to the visual length of the section, that they are attached to.
4.2.4
Overview of Train Visualization
When utilizing that trains are represented by having them placed next to the
track section, we can look at how a train is to be represented when the HTO-
value for an end changes. Table 4.1 shows the intended graphical representation,
when the HTO-value is set to a given value. Since the HTO-value is represented
by 3-bits, we can get 8 dierent states, where 5 of these are safe, while the rest
are insecure states, that violates the dened safety properties.
Table 4.1, however only shows the HTO value for a single direction. The other
directions of a track section may contribute to an unsafe state for the network,
which e.g. could be when a train is travelling in the opposite direction on the
same track section. If this is the case, we have trains moving in both directions
on a single track section, which is a clear violation of the safety properties, as
it would cause a head-on collision between the trains.

4.2.4.1
Point Sections
With this approach, we could easily handle the positioning of the trains on the
linear section. However looking at the point sections, we have an immediate
problem, as a point consist of three sub-sections that needs to represent the
visualizations shown in Table 4.1. To solve this, it has been decided to split
the visualization up into two parts, such that each representation is split in
half. This can cause some issues, as two states will look-a-like, namely when
==PAGE:68==
54
Visual Representation
HTO is set to one or two. This would generate the same look on a point-section,
however this is not a problem, as one is a safe state, while the other is an
error-state, and would be marked accordingly. Therefore it would be possible to
dierentiate between the two visualizations, as one is marked as an error-state,
while the other is not.
An example of this approach can be seen in Figure 4.21, in which we have a point
section, where a train is completely contained within. The given positioning of
the train is representing that the HTO-value for S2PM is 7, since both the head
and tail of the train is present at the point section.
Figure 4.21: Example of how trains are visualized on a point section. In this
case a complete train is heading from the STEM to PLUS
By having this representation, each sub-section of the point is able to support
a single part of the visualizations given in Table 4.1.
4.2.4.2
Error States
In case there are any violations of the safety properties for the network  e.g.
two trains collide in case they are on the same track section, we will highlight
the track sections, in which errors occur. An example of this highlighting can
be seen in Figure 4.22, in which two trains experience a head-on collision.
!
Figure 4.22: Track section with highlighted error sign
==PAGE:69==
4.2 State Visualization
55
4.2.5
Routes
As mentioned in Section 2.2.3.1, a route has an eect on the state of each
lockable element contained within a network, depending on the path it takes.
This can be utilized to display the state of each lockable element, as a route
tries to use them.
A lockable element may take three dierent states, depending on if a route is
using the given section or not. The three states were as previously mentioned
FREE, EXLCK and USED. Here FREE indicates that it is not used, while both EXLCK
and USED indicate that a route is using the section in some way.
Each of these states may be represented by a colour, indicating the internal
state of the lockable element.
FREE represent that no route is currently utilizing the element. The active
route through the section will be black, when the element is in this state.
This state represents the initial state for a lockable element.
EXLCK represent that the element has been locked by a route, in order for
it to be used. This simply means that it has been reserved  and we will
provide the element with a green colour (i.e. the active route through the
element will become green).
USED is set when a train is located on the element. This will result in the
element being coloured red (again it is the active route that will become
red).
An example of the colouring can be seen in Figure 4.23, in which multiple routes
are active.
Figure 4.23: Visualisation of lockable elements eected by routes
In the given example we have two trains, that might be in the process of passing
each other, such that the left-train is travelling in the UP direction, while the
right one is travelling in the DOWN direction. This would mean that the trains are
to pass each other at the given location. As seen in Figure 4.23, the left train is
already occupying the rest of its route, as both sections are red (indicating that
==PAGE:70==
56
Visual Representation
the sections are in the USED state). Meanwhile we have that the right train has
yet to enter the point and platform, but it has reserved the lockable elements,
by setting them in the EXLCK state, indicated by the green colour.
With this solution one can follow the state of each individual lockable element,
and potentially spot errors in the interlocking system.
In Figure 4.23, there is a single element, that is in the FREE state, namely the
left-most element  as it is coloured black.
4.3
Simulation
When running the model checker, one will in some cases get an counter-example
back, meaning that the verication of the interlocking system has failed. This
should somehow be visualised, and this is where the visual representation comes
into play, as described in Section 4.1 and Section 4.2. This visual representation
can be used to show the state that each object in the network has, as well as
include where trains are located.
However the user making the model would be interested in getting a quick
overview of what state each object in the network has. And as the model checker
will produce a table-like output, this can be hooked into a table visualising all
the values that has been provided by the model-checker. Since the model checker
will output a lot of lines, we will structure it, such that all information regarding
e.g. one track section is contained within a couple of lines of the table or make a
list under each track section, containing the values assigned to that track section
 enabling one to hide a given elements variables.
Figure 4.24, shows how this table could look like. The counter-example provided
in Figure 4.24 consists of two steps, in which step 2 is currently selected. The
state that can be derived from the attributes of the current step, will be utilized
by the network to visualize the state according to the denition stated previ-
ously. And for convenience, the current step will be highlighted in the column
that corresponds to the step, which in the case of Figure 4.24 is column 2, with
the grey background.
In the given example, shown in Figure 4.24, we have three linear track sections,
in which two, i.e. T10 and T14 are folded out, such that all variables for the
sections are shown.
While we for T12 has chosen to hide all the variables,
which enable one to put focus on some specic elements that one believe to be
the cause of the problem.
==PAGE:71==

4.4
Connector Model
As we need to model a railway network, one approach in how to structure the
network is by having the dierent track sections, act as a connections between
nodes, that represent the points in which the track sections are getting connected
to each other  similar to the approach used in Safecap. An example of this
approach can be seen in Figure 4.25, which could represent the layout for a
simple station.
Since all track sections are considered connections, we have added a special
item used as a connector, that is able to hook multiple connections together
(establishing neighbours for the track sections). These will act as the nodes
between the connections.
The connectors can be seen in Figure 4.25 as the
==PAGE:72==
58
Visual Representation
Figure 4.25: Connector Mode
circles, and the lines are the track sections.
4.4.1
Creation of Network
When creating a network utilising that every track section is a connection, one
has to rst place at least one node, that we may hook the connections onto.
When this rst node is placed, we may drag either linear- or point-sections out
of the initial node thereby creating a new track section.
When the connection, i.e. linear- or point-section is created it will connect to
either another node or create a new node, where the user decided he wanted the
end-point of the connection to be.
4.4.1.1
Adding Linear Section
When adding a linear section, one will simply have to drag it out from an pre-
existing node, and the DOWN side will then be determined to be the one connected
to the left-most node.
Figure 4.26: Adding linear track section to existing diagram
Figure 4.26 shows an example of how one would add a linear track section to a
network, when utilising the connector mode. First a connector is dragged out
from a node, visualised as the dashed line, and when one reaches the intended
end-point, a node will automatically be added at the given location, with the
new linear section established between the two nodes. However in case a free
node is already present at the given location, a connection will instead be created
==PAGE:73==
4.4 Connector Model
59
between these two nodes (a free node, is simply a node that has the possibility
to have more connections attached).
This make for a very simple work-ow for the user, as he can continuously add
more linear sections as he goes, as nodes are created on-the-y. So after the
initial node has been placed, one can add new linear track sections without
having to change the type that needs to be created.
4.4.1.2
Adding Point
Compared to adding a linear section, a point section is more complicated, as
it has a third end-point (which is the MINUS connection). It will be created
using the same work-ow, as for the linear track section, in which one drags a
connection out from a node. However the work-ow is only the same for the
STEM and PLUS end-point, which represent the linear part of the point section.
However, as we are dragging a connection, we have no control of the third end-
point, i.e. the MINUS part. For this reason there are two options on how to
handle the creation of the MINUS end of the point section.
New MINUS node One option is to have a third node created automatically,
when one has drawn the connection between STEM and PLUS. This newly
created node will then act as the MINUS node for the point, and it will be
placed over the PLUS end of the point.
Figure 4.27: Adding point to diagram without existing nodes
Connect MINUS to existing node Another option would be for the user to
select between existing nodes which allow for additional connections, i.e.
have less than 2 existing connections. Depending on the location of this
node, STEM and PLUS will be adjusted accordingly.
Figure 4.28 shows an example on how one would add a point when utilising
existing nodes as the MINUS end-point.
A pop-up with free nodes will
be available and the user will select one of the nodes  in the case of
Figure 4.28 the user selects c13, and the point-section is created between
c10, c12 and c13.
==PAGE:74==
60
Visual Representation
Available Minus End-Points
c11
c13
c10
c11
c12
c13
Figure 4.28: Adding point to diagram with existing nodes
The second method, that is connecting to an already existing node have the
drawback, that the node that needs to contain the MINUS end of the point has
to be created beforehand. For this reason it would make sense to combine the
two methods, such that the user has the option to either create a new node or
attach the MINUS end to an already existing node.
4.4.2
Editing of Network
When editing a network, one question comes to mind  what should happen
when one deletes a track section, i.e. a connection. Should we remove the nodes
if no other section is connected to it or let it be.
4.4.2.1
Keep nodes
By keeping all nodes when deleting the track section, we ensure that the outline
of the previous network is kept intact. However in many cases, when one edits a
network, a new outline is also preferred, so old nodes are not necessary needed.
==PAGE:75==
4.5 Node Model
61
Utilising that no nodes are deleted, when the connections are removed, will result
in a tedious work-ow for the user, in case he removed multiple connections. In
that case, it will leave a lot of unused nodes in the diagram, which he will have
to remove in a separate step.
4.4.2.2
Delete nodes
In case all nodes gets deleted after the last connection to it is removed, one
will lose the layout that has been constructed with the nodes. However, if one
has deleted all connections to a single node, it is most likely not to be used in
the modied network. Therefore it would make sense to delete a node from the
network, when it is no longer being used.
Combine the two methods
However one could combine the two methods,
such that the user has an option of keeping the nodes, when all connections are
removed to it. This could be a pop-up presented to the user, stating whether
a lonely node should be deleted with the removal of a connection. The same
could be done when an end-point of a connection is switched, leaving a node
with no connections. Asking the user, if all unused nodes should be removed
will help in maintaining a network in a desired state, and thereby helping the
user in keeping the network clean of unused elements.
4.5
Node Model
Another method for creating and maintaining a diagram showing a railway
network, is to have each of the sections be a node in itself. This means that one
would have to place the desired track sections rst, and then later dening their
neighbours (by connecting them). An example of the end result, when utilizing
this method can be seen in Figure 4.29, in which a couple of linear sections and
point sections, shows what could be a train station. And as one can see the
dierent sections act as their own component, connected to the other sections
with a dashed line (and each section will have several ports, representing the
ends of the section)
The connections between the dierent section are done by connecting the dif-
ferent anchor/ports that are present on a track section. These connections are
shown as dashed lines in Figure 4.29, and have no other use than to dene
==PAGE:76==
62
Visual Representation
Figure 4.29: Node mode example
which anchors are connected to each other, thereby stating which sections are
neighbours.
4.5.1
Creation of Network
When using this method, the creation process of a network is quite straightfor-
ward as the creator would have to place the desired nodes containing the sections
and connect them at the end or as he goes. A problem that occurs, when one
uses this method, is that one has to manually connect each anchor/port on the
node with an anchor/port of another node, unless it represents an endpoint of
the network.
4.5.1.1
Adding Linear Section
Linear sections can with ease be added to a diagram, as it will simply be a
node with two anchors that need to be connected to other sections. The only
interesting factor, when adding a linear track section is in what direction DOWN
and UP is placed. There are two possible solution on how to control in which
end, these are located.
Pre-determined As the linear track section is straight, one can see the left
side as the DOWN side and the right side as the UP side of the section. This
however means that linear sections are not allowed to be ipped or tilted
in any way. Or if it is ipped, the sides must change.
Dynamic This utilise that the user may change which side is seen as the UP and
DOWN end. If this method was used, checks must be enforced that ensure
that two UP sides are not connected to each other, and the same must be
ensured for the DOWN sides. A possibility could be, that one would assign
a reference point to the network, which is used to determine the DOWN and
UP for each section.
==PAGE:77==
4.5 Node Model
63
Otherwise the adding of a linear track section is straight forward, as one simply
has to place the component anywhere on the diagram and connect its anchors
to other nodes  such that we have a similar result as in Figure 4.29, in which
multiple linear sections has been added.
4.5.1.2
Adding Point
Adding a point is straight forward, as one places a node containing the desired
layout for the point, and afterwards connect the anchors of the node to another
track section within the network.
Figure 4.30: Adding point existing diagram
Figure 4.30 depicts the situation in which a point is added  shown as the
point with red highlighting. The PLUS and MINUS anchors of this node is then
connected to an already created point, thereby stating that the new point is
a continuation of the old point (two point sections would most likely not be
connected to each other in a network-layout).
Approaches for adding a new Point
When adding a point section, there
are two approaches one could take, when utilizing the node model.
Preset Nodes One approach would be to have the four dierent congurations
for a point section, see Section 4.1.2, given as nodes that can be added
to the network. This mean that a person would have to switch between
the dierent congurations of a point section when creating a network (if
dierent point sections are needed).
Single Node Another approach would be to have a single node that can be
placed in the network, and one can modify which conguration the point
section should use, after it has been deployed. This would be done by
changing the location of the MINUS anchor, after the node has been created.
As the MINUS anchor is moved around, the STEM and PLUS anchors will
switch accordingly, such that it will not violate the properties of a point
section. The dierent position that the MINUS anchor can be placed at will
be xed to certain locations.
==PAGE:78==
64
Visual Representation
These approaches both have advantages and disadvantages, depending on what
the user wishes to do. For the preset nodes, one has to change between the
nodes in order to place new point sections in dierent congurations. Where
we for the single node method can place as many points as we intend, and after
the placement adjust, which conguration of a point section that will be used.
Being able to correct a point sections conguration on-the-y, will give an easier
work-ow, as one would not have to delete a node and add another to change
the conguration.
A third option could be to combine the two methods, such that the user of
the editor could choose the work-style that suits him the best. And most im-
portantly giving the option of adjusting a point sections conguration after
creation.
4.5.2
Editing of Network
Editing a network is quite easy, when utilizing that every track section is a node.
This means that in case one wants to make a change in the network, a node
can simply be removed  which in turn will remove all references/connections
that the removed node had to other sections. After this, one can add a new
track section to the network, and connect it to any other track section within
the network (that accepts more neighbours).
Figure 4.31: Example of removal of track section when utilising Node mode
==PAGE:79==
4.6 Comparison of Models
65
Figure 4.31, shows a small example of how one would edit an existing network.
As it can be see, we remove the right most point from the network. By removing
the point, all connections are removed instantly. This means that the other track
sections that the point was connected to can be used by other track sections 
either by connecting it to an already existing track section or create new sections
for it to be connected to.
In the example shown in Figure 4.31, we have however chosen to add a new
point to the right.
The process of adding a new points has been explained
earlier. However, as it can be seen it is quite easy to edit an existing diagram,
which can be used to alter a network to show a dierent scenario.
4.6
Comparison of Models
When looking at the two methods of constructing a network, i.e. the Connector
Model and Node Model, and try to compare them to see which will provide the
best work-ow. The comparison will look at how each model performs from a
users perspective  and we will focus on the two major operations, one will use
when working with a network-layout, namely constructing and maintaining the
network.
4.6.1
Creation of a Network
When looking at how one would create a network using the two methods, we
have that with the Connector Model, the user can continuously add more and
more track sections without changing tool, as the nodes will be created as one
places the connections (track sections). This can be achieved after a single node
has been placed.
Looking at the Node Model on the other hand, the process of making the outline
of the track will take as many steps, as for the Connector Model. However after
having placed all the nodes, one will have to manually connect the track sections
(nodes) in the correct manor. This will be quite tedious in case the network is
complex.
Another positive feature about the Connector Model is that one can freely move
around the dierent nodes, re-shaping the network as one pleases. Where we for
the Node Model are more pre-determined in what shapes, we have available, as
the anchor points of the nodes are xed to stay inside the nodes borders. How-
==PAGE:80==
66
Visual Representation
ever for a person editing a network, one should avoid having a too complicated
diagram in order to reduce confusion, when looking at the network-layout. In
this way the Node Model helps as it keeps the dierent track section contained
 ensuring that each track section has identical proportions, and has a better
chance of corresponding to what a domain expert would expect.
One major drawback about the Connector Model, is the lack of extendibility, in
case one would have to dene a new track section. If one would add a new track
section, one would have to dene in code, how many sub-section are contained
in the given section, and decide how these should be connected to nodes. For the
Node Model on the other hand, it is much more streamlined, as one can dene
the internal looks of the node, and where the anchor/ports are to be placed on
the boundaries. So in terms of the requirement, that the environment should
be easy to extend, the Node Model is surely desired.
4.6.2
Editing of Network
When looking at the editing of a network, we have that the Node Model will
function best, when modifying a network as each track section is a component
in it self.
So it is clear what will be removed from the network, when one
deletes a single track section. Whereas we for the Connector Model, have a hard
time determining, when the nodes in which the track sections are connected to,
should be deleted or not. This could lead to annoyance for the user, if he would
have to answer whether a lone node should be removed, whenever such a node
is established.
However exchanging a component, i.e. either a linear track section or point with
the opposite, is almost the same approach for both methods. As we in the Node
Model, simply replaces the old node with a new and connects the anchors to
the needed track sections. Where we for the Connector Model, simply remove
the old connection and draw a new connection in its place. However the Node
Model will require a few more steps by the user, as he will have to connect the
anchors, where we for the Connector Model have them connected whenever the
user draws the connections.
4.6.3
Conclusion
When looking at the advantages and disadvantages of both methods, it is clear
that the work-ow for the Node Model is easier for an user to gure out. This
is due to the fact, that you with ease can see what will be deleted when a track
==PAGE:81==
4.6 Comparison of Models
67
section is removed from the network. Instead of the Connector Model, where one
should utilise a lot of pop-ups, to ensure that only the parts that the users wants
removed are in fact removed. So from a users perspective, the Node Model will
provide a considerable easier work-ow, where there is no need for any pop-ups
clarifying what the intention of an user action is in some cases.
So even though, the work-ow for the Node Model may be tedious in some parts,
as the user will have to manually connect the nodes anchors to other nodes. It is
still preferable, as the generated pop-ups in the Connector Model, would annoy
the user when working with the environment.
All in all the Node Model is preferable, as it will x the layout of the network
in such a way, that one would easily get an overview of how a network is struc-
tured.
This is due to, the nodes having the track sections contained within
its boundaries, and the anchors can only be moved to xed locations, thereby
dening a predetermined look for all sections. Opposed to the Connector Model
in which the user may place the nodes which the track sections are connected
to, at any place in the diagram  which could lead to a confusing layout for the
network.
However the most important requirement is that the environment, should be
easy to extend with new types of track sections. This is hard to achieve with
the Connector Model, as one would have to correct internally in the environment,
how a specic section should be constructed and work. For the Node Model, this
is not the case, since it will contain all functionality for a given track section
within the boundaries of a node  giving us a more general approach on how to
dene new types. Due to the generality of the Node Model, it is the best choice
to be used for the environment.
==PAGE:82==
==PAGE:83==
Chapter 5
Work-ows
An editor will have to be created, that support the graphical domain-specic
language described in Chapter 4. For this reason, we will need to describe the
work-ow for the most common tasks, that should be performed, when using
the editor. These work-ows are related to the creation and maintenance of
network-layouts.
The work-ows should both be user-friendly and intuitive, ensuring that the
editor is easy to work with. For this purpose, ve work-ows has to be consid-
ered, and they will mostly revolve around inserting track sections and modifying
these after their placement.
Create Will describe the process of inserting a new track section into an ex-
isting network-layout. This includes placing the track sections at a given
location and modifying it. See Section 5.1 for more details on this work-
ow.
Connect Is the process of dening the neighbours of the track section, that has
been placed within the network-layout. See Section 5.2 for more details
about this work-ow.
Move Describes what will happen when a track section is moved to a new
location within a network. See Section 5.3 for more details on this work-
ow.
==PAGE:84==
70
Work-ows
Delete Indicates what will happen when a selection of track sections are deleted
from the network-layout. See Section 5.3 for more details on this work-
ow.
Disconnect Describes the work-ow of removing a connection between two
track sections, such that they no longer are considered neighbours. See
Section 5.4, for more details on this work-ow.
These work-ows represent the main functionality of when a person tries to
create and maintain any network, he intends to verify against the model-checker.
5.1
Create Track Section
The ability to create new track sections is a key-feature of the editor, as it grants
the user the ability to actually extend a network (and create one to begin with).
As it is a work-ow, that will be used a lot, it should therefore be easy to create
new track sections.
The process of creating a new track section is quite straightforward, as it can be
seen in Figure 5.1, which shows an activity diagram presenting the work ow.
The intended work-ow, is that the user selects which track section, he would
like to create from a selection of implemented track sections.
After the user has selected a track section, he clicks anywhere on the network-
layout, and the given track section will be placed at the position provided by
the user.
When the user has placed the track section, he has the option to move the
anchors on the track section, such that it has another conguration than the
default (in case the anchors has valid locations). This could e.g. be a point
section, in which the MINUS end has to be moved to another valid location. It
will not be possible to move all anchors, as some are locked to a specic position,
i.e. they have no valid locations, other than their default location. Beside being
able to move anchors, one will also be able to adjust the parameters, e.g. the
length of the track section.
When the user is satised with the result, he will simply have to deselect the
created track section, and he has successfully added a new track section to the
network. At a later time, he may adjust a track section by simply selecting it
in the network.
==PAGE:85==

5.2
Connect Track Sections
A central concept of railway networks is that track sections are connected, in
order to establish a complex network, consisting of several track sections. In
order to accomplish this, a connect work-ow has to be established, i.e. how
would a user connect two track sections (such that they are seen as neighbours).
5.2.1
Current
The current work-ow is probably not the most user-friendly, however it gets
the job done. Currently, a user will have to select a tool, that is able to connect
two anchors together  stating that the track sections that own the anchors are
now neighbours.
==PAGE:86==
72
Work-ows
All in all, this work-ow is not the best, as the ow of the connecting several
anchors are quite time-consuming, as the tool will have be selected for each
connection to be made.
This is the reason, why an intended work-ow has been proposed, which could
be implemented if time allows it.
5.2.2
Intended
The intended work-ow that would be implemented, if time allows it, can be
seen as an activity diagram in Figure 5.2.

5.3 Move Track Sections
73
The idea behind this work-ow is that one selects an anchor on a track section.
This anchor is then moved to an anchor on another track section, and in case
a connection may be created between the two anchors (they are both free, in
opposite site of the track section etc.) it will be created, when the user decides
that the two anchor are to be connected.
As an extra feature, one could allow the connected track sections to be placed
next to each other (so the user wont have to move them afterwards).
This
however should only be optional, as the user may not always want the two track
sections placed next to each other.
In case the anchor was not free or other violations was present, a connection
between the two anchors would not be created  and the network would stay
unchanged.
5.3
Move Track Sections
As the initial position of a track section may not be nal, it has to be decided
what will happen, whenever a user decides to move a track section.
5.3.1
Current
The current work-ow is as one would expect  as moving a track section will
simply cause the given track section to be moved to the new location.
Every component that is somehow attached to the track section, will also be
moved accordingly to the new location (so they stay attached to the track sec-
tion).
However the neighbours for the track section will not be moved, unless they
were part of the selection (one may move multiple track sections at once).
5.3.2
Intended
Even though the basic work-ow, provides the feature one wants, the work-ow
can somewhat be extended, to give the user more options, when moving a given
==PAGE:88==

Work-ows
track section. The work-ow that was intended, when moving a track section,
can be seen as an activity diagram in Figure 5.3.

This would however be annoying, in case one would simply have to move a single
track section to t better into the network-layout. It would therefore be bene-
cial, that one could turn this feature on and o, to enable quick disconnecting.

5.4
Disconnect Track Sections
When disconnecting two track section from each other, one will simply have to
select the connection between the anchors that handles the relationship between
the track sections. The connection is represented by a dashed line. And when
selected, one simply deletes the connection, and thereby deleting the relationship
between the two track section.
Another possibility for disconnecting track sections from each other, would as
stated in Section 5.3 be to disconnect them when one of the track sections are
moved. This would be if both were not part of the moved selection.

A user may wants to delete some of the track sections, he has placed in the
editor, in order to minimize the network or simply replace track sections. For
the user to be able to delete track sections in a network, he will need to follow

Chapter 6
Design
In order to full the requirements for the environment, we will have to make
some decisions in regards to the design of the environment, such that we with
ease will be able to full the given requirements.
Beside general decisions,
the core model of the environment will be provided, which will be used as the
ecore model for EMF  the framework we choose to develop the environment
in. Furthermore details on how to achieve extendibility for the environment is
highlighted.
6.1
Extendibility
One of the requirements for the environment, was that it should be easy to
extend with new elements  such as sections and extensions. When we are using
EMF/GMF for the development, as chosen in Chapter 3, we have two immediate
choices that can be utilized to full this requirement.
Extension Points One approach would be to have several extension points,
that can be used to append extra functionality onto the editor. The editor
would then set up the given skeleton, that is needed in order to support
the extension points.
==PAGE:92==
78
Design
In order to use extension points in the environment, several interface would
have to be declared, that the extensions that uses the extension points has
to implement. An example of such an interface, could be one dening a
track section, that must be extended in order to supply new track sections
for the environment. Instead of an interface, one can also dene abstract
classes that may be extended  such that some core functionality is already
dened for an extension.
A disadvantage for choosing to use extension points, is the development,
as extending the environment, would require implementing new classes
that extends the extension point. This means one would have to produce
a new plug-in each time a new type has to included. This will take time,
but may oer great exibility in what is possible, as long as the complexity
of the interface supports the needed behaviour.
Therefore one must ensure that the interface or abstract, enables one to
describe any needed behaviour. So a great deal of the limitation of using
this method for extending an environment, is that one has to carefully
describe the needed interface/abstract class.
An issue that could arise, when utilizing this method, could be that a
person potentially breaks the environment, by providing faulty code that
causes exception to be thrown. This is as the developer would be able to
use any method available in JAVA.
XML Conguration A dierent approach, would be to utilize XML to dene
a conguration for the environment. The conguration would be specied
with the help of a schema, that ensures that one is unable to create illegal
denitions for the editor.
Utilizing XML makes it easy for a user to extend the given environment
with new types (i.e. sections and extensions). This is due to the user,
only being able to dene elements that corresponds to what has been
dened valid in the provided schema. This means that one can extend the
environment, by adjusting the content of a XML le, and it prohibits one
from writing any code to the environment.
As the user may not create any code for the environment, the addition
of new types cannot cause the environment to behave strangely, since the
environment has been suited to handle anything that is to be stated within
the XML conguration. And as the conguration follows a schema, illegal
congurations will not be accepted by the environment. It is therefore
safer to use XML for the purpose of extending the environment with new
types.
Even though XML may be easier to work with, it has the disadvantages
that one cannot change the dynamic behaviour of the dierent element
 if this is not dened in the XML schema one has available. If changes
==PAGE:93==
6.2 Overall Design
79
would have to made to the schema, one would with high probability have
to make changes in the core of the environment  as new features would
have to be included.
Both approaches has advantages and disadvantages. The XML on one hand
has the advantages that a specic schema has been dened, meaning an illegal
denition cannot be made. However XML lacks some expressiveness, as one can
only utilize what has been dened in the XML schema. This is where extension
points could be utilized instead, as one may utilize the internal features of
JAVA for dening new elements. However we are still limited by the interface
that will be provided by the extension points. Therefore an excessive interface
would have to be dened in order for the extension point to be useful, and not
miss features that a developer would require to implement certain elements.
An disadvantages of allowing anyone to write code for the environment, is the
chance that one writes incorrect code  that causes the environment not to
behave as intended.
With this said, we still went with utilizing XML to dene a conguration that
allows for extending the editor. This was done as it has the advantages that one
does not need to have knowledge about a given language, but can simply dene
new elements, according to the dened schema. The downside however is, that
one is conned to what is possible within the schema.
The developer of the framework, may however extend the given schema in case
new features need to be added.
This means that the amount of developers
needed to support the framework is limited, and persons dening extra elements,
need not know of the internal structure of the environment.
For more about how the extendibility has been dened, see Section 6.5 in which
the used schema is dened.
6.2
Overall Design
The environment to be created should be easy to extend with both new features
and element types (that may be placed in the network). New element types was
decided to be included through an XML conguration, as stated in Section 6.1.
This decision has a great impact, on how the environment is to be constructed.
The environment is to consist of several components, that will interact with each
other to give the desired functionality. In Figure 6.1, one can see the dierent
components that the environment will consist of.
==PAGE:94==
80
Design
One noticeable component is the Simulation, which is placed outside the Editor.
The purpose of this component is to handle all communication with the existing
tool-chain, meaning our editor will have no knowledge about how its constructed
network is used.
Furthermore, it was chosen to place the routes in the simulation component,
as this may not be a feature that another tool would need. This separation was
done, to ensure that the constructed editor would be usable for other tools with
little change to the internal structure. And it was deemed that only the network,
would be interesting for other tools, that might consider using the editor.
Any component outside the Editor can modify any data contained within the
network specied for the editor - through the provided interface.
FileHandling
Edit
Diagram
Configuration

Given a general idea about the relations between the dierent components of
Figure 6.1, we can explain each component in detail  including the purpose of
each component. The top-components as shown in Figure 6.1 are:
==PAGE:95==
6.2 Overall Design
81
Editor The editor is responsible for constructing the network-layout contained
in the interlocking system. It would be possible to utilize the editor for
other purposes than the interlocking system  as it describes the graphical
denition for a train-network.
The component consists of several sub-
components that handle dierent aspects of the editor, see Section 6.2.1,
for more details about these sub-components.
Simulation The simulation is the component that is responsible for handling
communication with the existing tool-chain. It will also contain the de-
nition for the interlocking table, needed for the verication in the existing
tool-chain (the interlocking table was placed here, as it is specic for the
given tool-chain).
For more information about the component and its
sub-components, see Section 6.2.2.
FileHandling The le handling oers an extension point, that will be used to
save any relevant information in an XML le, that may be utilized by the
tool-chain (or any other tool). Information about this component, can be
found in Section 6.2.3.
Existing Tool-Chain Represents the external tool-chain that is used to con-
duct the verication. Only the simulation component knows of this tool
 meaning we have a separation of concern, since that editor will focus on
creating networks, and allow for displaying the element states, while the
simulator handles all external communication.
Having the environment constructed this way, we have achieved some form of
separation of concern  where each component handles a unique feature of the
environment. This gives us the exibility needed to support easy extendibil-
ity, when it comes to functionality, as one can simply hook into the Editor
component, in order to utilize the network constructed within the environment.
6.2.1
Editor
The editor is as stated, responsible for creating and maintaining a network
layout. For this it has several sub-components, that enables it to do just that.
As we have decided to create the editor with the help of EMF/GMF, the design
is based heavily on the usage of the said frameworks.
This means we have three sub-components that is directly linked to EMF/GMF.
Core This contain the core-features of the editor. It contains the denitions
of each element, that must be contained in a network, as dened in the
==PAGE:96==
82
Design
ecore-diagram for EMF. It is EMF that generates the base structure for
this component, which is then modied to suit the intended working of
the environment.
If any internal change is to happen to an instance of an object, it must
go through the core component  executed by commands. The domain
model used to generate this component is described in Section 6.3.
Edit Contains utility functionality for the editor, mostly in relation to dene
which properties each object should have displayed (in the property view).
Diagram Denes the graphical appearance of the elements, that is dened in
the core. This is the graphical front-end of the editor, and may be used
to modify the internal structure of the network. Not all objects dened in
the core, need to have a graphical appearance, as some instances are only
created to handle some work behind the scene, such as storing relevant
data.
These three sub-components is the basis for the editor, as they provide the core-
functionality and the graphical denitions for the features contained in the core.
With just these three sub-component, we would have a simple editor, that could
create networks.
However as one can see, we have one extra sub-component, namely the Con-
figuration, which as the name suggest is used to congure a network, to t
the needs dened with the conguration le (e.g. which sections it should be
possible to create).
6.2.1.1
Conguration
The conguration sub-component, has the task to congure the editor, such that
it has the types of element needed to construct a given network. As decided in
Section 6.1, the extendibility should be handled with the use of a XML schema,
and a corresponding XML conguration le.
This means that the component must be able to read the given XML les and
hold what information is available for the editor. Whenever an element is to
be constructed, it will be managed by this sub-component, as it has access to
the conguration for any type, that may be included in the network. The sub-
component will set up the element according to the dened conguration. See
more in Section 6.6 about the conguration sub-component.
==PAGE:97==
6.2 Overall Design
83
6.2.2
Simulation
As stated, we have chosen to separate all communication with the existing tool-
chain to a component of its own.
This means that the editor, will have no
knowledge of the current state of the execution, other than the counter-examples
provided to it by the simulation.
This component, will also dene the interlocking table, which will consist of the
routes associated with a network. The routes are currently a xed entity with
specic entries. This could be corrected, such that they become more dynamic,
and would need a specication, that informs what entities should be included.
However, the simulation will run the existing tool-chain with the information it
gets from the editor and from its own route table  and pass the results from
the tool-chain back to the editor to be displayed.
6.2.3
FileHandling
This will be responsible for saving to an XML le (used by the external tool
in our case). It will save any element that extends the given extension point,
which currently consist of two methods, as seen in Figure 6.2.
«interface»

In the current state, we will only support saving to XML les, however at a
later state, one could include the possibility to load a specication given as a
XML le into any component, that extends the given extension point. If this
were to be supported, the provided interface would have to be extended, such
that it supports a load-method.
The interface given in Figure 6.2, has the save method, which takes a document
(an internal representation of the XML le in JAVA) and editor (the editor, that
contains a given network). As we utilize Eclipse and EMF/GMF, the editor that
contains the network may be fetched by any plug-in, by locking at the active
editor of Eclipse.

6.3 Domain Model
85
As stated we use Eclipse, and we therefore utilize features related to the exten-
sion point implemented. One feature that can be used, is to quickly localize all
objects that extends any given extension point, through a registry that is present
within Eclipse. When all related objects are found, they get instantiated, giv-
ing us the opportunity to call the concrete instance, with any of the methods
dened by the interface in Figure 6.2, i.e. either getting the XML element to
be saved, or the index the given object should have within the XML le. The
index is important, as it determines in which order the generated elements will
be placed in the XML le. When all extensions has been called, we create the
XML le, and append all the elements (sorted according to their index) on the
provided path.
6.3
Domain Model
The domain model of the solution, will be contained in the core sub-component
of the editor component. This is what establish the foundation of the ecore
model, that will be used by EMF to generate the structure of the solution (which
will be modied to suit with the design decisions).
6.3.1
Network
The network is responsible for gathering the information regarding the rail-
network that we intend to model on. For this reason, it must be able to get
information on all parts of the network. Figure 6.4 shows a representation of
what is contained within a network. All references is a composition to ensure
that everything related to the network will be deleted whenever the network is
deleted (or stored when it is saved).
The network itself does not have much functionality beside being responsible for
keeping track of the objects, that are dened in the network itself  and locating
these with its methods. The network mostly act as a container for all the other
objects, and will be the top-level object of the editor.
If we take a look at some of the object, that a network will contain, we have
several dened. One of these are the Labels which is used to represent textual
information for either TrackSections or Extension, and enables the user to
adjust information by altering it directly from the label. It was decided to
place these as children of the network  as they should be represented as a
top-level node in the GMF diagram. If GMF were not to be used, they would

probably not be stored in the network, but rather under each individual object.
See Section 6.3.9 for more details about labels.
The Connection shown in Figure 6.4 will act as the connectors between the
TrackSections of the network  these will be connected to two anchors (see
Section 6.3.5). They will ensure that each TrackSection gets its neighbours
dened  without violating any safety properties.
The PropertyLookup is intended to store all user-dened properties for the
object-instances that is contained in the network  this excludes the properties
stored on a object, e.g the ID of the element. This enables objects to be extended
with extra properties (through the conguration), and they may be used to
dene the behaviour that the object must have.
See Section 6.3.2 for more
details on how the properties are handled.
In Section 6.3.3, details about the TrackSections are highlighted, however they
are more or less responsible for containing all visible parts of the network, as
they describe the visual layout of the given network.
The other graphical part of the network, namely the Extensions, are used to
describe the extra features that may be appended to sections, e.g. markerboards.
See Section 6.3.7 for more details about the Extensions.
6.3.1.1
External Objects
As the editor will save the network in a local le, and store what is modelled
in the network, we will need an option to let external plug-ins save information
==PAGE:101==
6.3 Domain Model
87
associated with the network.
This option needs to be included, as external
plug-ins may create objects that has some relation to a network, such as the
interlocking tables that is to be created in the simulation components.
For this purpose, we have the ExternalObjects, that represent data from any
plug-in, such that the plug-in may retrieve the stored data, when the network
is opened in the editor. This means we will have all data related to a Network,
stored in a single le, instead of having a separate les for each plug-in that
extends upon the environment.
Each ExternalObject will consist of two variables:
application This is a unique identier, given by a plug-in, and could be routes,
for storing the interlocking table created in the simulation plug-in.
context This is a string, representing a serialization of any object that a plug-in
needs to have stored. XML could be utilized for this serialization.
Any plug-in may store multiple entries, as long as it uses dierent IDs for the
data to be stored.
Whenever a network is opened, the plug-ins that may have stored data, as
ExternalObjects, must be informed that the network has been opened. How
this can be achieved, is shown in Section 7.6.
6.3.2
Properties
As mentioned in Section 6.3.1, a feature that allows for some extendibility of
the objects of a network, is the possibility to add extra properties to an object.
This is supported through the PropertyLookup, seen in Figure 6.5, which is a
global look-up table, containing the extra properties for an object (stored using
the objects ID). The PropertyLookup, consist of a map, that utilizes the ID of
the objects, to store and retrieve the properties related to the object.
Given an ID, the map may then have a PropertyMap stored, which is a mapping
of each property into a specic value. By using maps, to store the properties,
we ensure that only unique properties will be present for any object.
The use of ID as the rst key, also ensure that only a single object, may have
the given ID. This can be achieved by looking at the map, to see whether a
==PAGE:102==
88
Design
PropertyValue
value: String
defaultValue: String
useDefault: Boolean
show: Boolean
editable: Boolean
property: String
PropertyMap
PropertyLookup
property
1
ID
1
Figure 6.5: Class diagram for Properties
given ID is occupied within the map. If there already exist an entry with a
given ID, it means that another object has the ID, and one should be hindered
from using the provided ID. This simply means that whenever one tries to use
an ID, whether by manually changing it or if it is automatically generated, it
should always check whether the entry in the PropertyLookup is not taken
for the given ID  and if it is occupied, the ID for the object should not be
changed. This ensures that we will have unique IDs within any network, unless
an object states that the ID may be shared (this is true for some extensions,
as mentioned in Section 6.3.7.1).
6.3.2.1
PropertyValue
The property, that will be stored in an entry of the PropertyMap, is not just a
single value, representing the given property. The value of the property will be
stored as a string, as any object can be transformed to a string (as one could
serialize them, or in case of primitive types, simply convert them).
Beside the value of the property itself, the PropertyValue will also contain
the name of the property itself (used when one only have the PropertyValue
available, and not which entry is used). The PropertyValue will also include a
default value and whether the default value should be used. This enables one to
reset the state of the network, by setting every PropertyValue to their default
value, if stated by the useDefault value. Choosing not to use the default value
is important for some properties, in case they represent an internal state of the
object, such as where the anchors are located.
Finally we have two variables, that may be used to specify how the given prop-
erty may be used.
==PAGE:103==
6.3 Domain Model
89
show states whether it should be shown in the property view, that will list all
properties within a given PropertyMap, that has set this variable to true.
editable indicates whether the given property may have its value changed man-
ually  either by using a label or in the property view. Only properties
that has set this to true may be created as labels.
By having these PropertyValues, one may extend an object with as many
properties as one pleases, making the editor that much more extendible.
6.3.3
Track Sections
TrackSections are the backbone of the whole network, as they represent the
layout for the network. The TrackSections themselves will have all information
regarding the simulation contained within them, and each TrackSection will
represent a single section of the network, i.e. linear section, points section etc.
In Figure 6.6, the domain model of the TrackSection can be seen, and it
contains all necessary information needed to construct the visual representation
of the TrackSection.
A TrackSection holds internally both its position and its dimension (opposed
to only have the information available in the graphical editor). This is done, so
other objects with ease can utilize this information (for placing it in a relative
position to the TrackSection).

The TrackSection, is build up of multiple ConnectionPoints, that will be
used by the VisualSections to describe the layout of the TrackSection. The
ConnectionPoints, can be two dierent types, namely InternalConnection-
Points or AnchorConnectionPoints, and in Figure 6.7 it is displayed how these
ConnectionPoints, may be placed within the boundaries of a TrackSection.

Figure 6.7, shows a conguration for a point section, where we have six Anchor-

three VisualSections.
6.3.3.1
ConnectionPoints
The connections points is what the TrackSection, utilizes to construct its layout
(with the help of VisualSections, see Section 6.3.3.2). A ConnectionPoint
has a unique ID, such that it can be found within a TrackSection, and with
ease be referenced.
Moreover any ConnectionPoint, must have a location within the boundaries
of the TrackSection, and the abstract method getLocation(), will provide the
location of the ConnectionPoint (how the location is calculated depends on the
concrete type of ConnectionPoint). This is also the reason why a Connection-
Point has a reference to the TrackSection, it is contained within, as any con-
crete type will need the location and dimension of the TrackSection to calculate
their own location.
==PAGE:105==
6.3 Domain Model
91
We currently have two concrete ConnectionPoints:
InternalConnectionPoint Seen in Figure 6.6, it has two attributes that de-
cides the location of the given point. As one might have guessed, we have
that an InternalConnectionPoint, will lie within the boundaries of a
TrackSection. The position is given as percentages of the height (given
as y) and width (given as x) of the TrackSection, to ensure that we main-
tain the same layout in case the TrackSection is resized. In Figure 6.7,
a single InternalConnectionPoint is shown, which is the black circle in
the middle.
The percentages given as the position is always in relation to the Track-
Sections upper-left corner.
AnchorConnectionPoint These points represent locations, which Anchors
may be placed at (if part of their valid locations).
More information
about how their location gets specied is described in Section 6.3.5. In
Figure 6.7 they are shown as white circles, and as seen they are always
located at the border of the TrackSection (giving an idea on how their
location is to be specied).
The given Anchors of the TrackSection, is as mentioned placed at the location
of a AnchorConnectionPoint. The Anchors themselves determines the posi-
tions on the TrackSection, in which other TrackSections may be connected
to the TrackSection.
6.3.3.2
Visual Section
The VisualSections is what displays the layout of a particular TrackSection,
as it describes small sub-sections of the TrackSection.
A VisualSection will as shown in Figure 6.7, be placed between two Connec-
tionPoints, either internal or anchor, and the VisualSection will update its
position, whenever these points are moved.
However as seen in Figure 6.8, a VisualSection is more than a simple straight
line. One has several ways of attaching external gures to the VisualSection,
e.g. to visualize the location of trains present on the TrackSection. This is
done with the help of either FigureExtensionAreas or ExtendableLocations,
which allows for external gures/extensions to be attached to a VisualSection
at a given location.

The FigureExtensionArea, denes a non-resizeable area, used to dene an area
next to a VisualSection, in which a gure may be placed. See Section 6.3.4
for more information regarding the FigureExtensionArea.
The ExtendableLocation, denes a single location on the given VisualSection,
that an extension may be attached to. See Section 6.3.4 for more information
regarding the denition of an ExtendableLocation.
In order to visualize the states that a lockable element (i.e. the TrackSection)
may take, one will have to be able to dene the colour each VisualSection may
have. This is achieved by having a ColorValue on each VisualSection, that
denes the RGB-colour, that the section must have in the current state. Any
VisualSection, will have an active and default ColorValue, where the default
is used when the element is placed, or the state is reset. Where the active is
what will be displayed when the state for the lockable element is changed.
Any ColorValue, can also state whether the VisualSection, should be visible,
which can be utilize to visualize the intermediate state of a point section (as
these will only have to be visible in a small sub-set of the states).
Example
To illustrate how a VisualSection would look like, a small example
has been provided, as seen in Figure 6.9.
The example in Figure 6.8, shows the representation of a point section, and as
we can see we have one internal point, that makes the connections between the
three anchors. So a point section, consist of three visual sections, in which all
have two FigureExtensionAreas attached to it (which will be responsible for
holding the visual representation of trains). The given FigureExtensionArea,

In the example, we have no ExtendableLocations, as these should only occur

6.3.4
Locations
Any Location, denes a position in the given network, that allows for exten-
sions or any visual gure to appear. Either by dening a single location, which
extensions may be attached to, or an area that may be used by a gure.
It was decided to allow for having gures attached to both TrackSections and
VisualSection, each dened by dierent concrete instances of a Location.
Both a TrackSection and VisualSection, support a single location that may
be used to place an Extension at (containing the Figure to be placed at the
location).
However, VisualSections may also provide areas that an gure, may be con-
tained within, as seen in Figure 6.9.
As seen in Figure 6.10, we have dened three concrete Location types, that
represents a unique situations.
Each dierent Location has an ID, used to
identify and localize a Location  while the specic placement of the Location
will be determined by the concrete types.

6.3.5
Anchors
The Anchors are what is used to connect the TrackSections in a network to-
gether. They dene the points on the TrackSections in which a Connection
may be established with another TrackSections (thereby dening the neigh-
bours for the TrackSections).

Figure 6.12: Class diagram for Anchors
As seen in Figure 6.12, an Anchor, is related to several AnchorConnection-
Point, which all have dierent meanings in relation to the Anchor. The current
point, denotes the location the Anchor is currently located at, while the default
point is the location in which the Anchor starts. These may be dierent in case
the Anchor has several valid positions it may be placed at. The valid positions,
allows the user to move the position of an Anchor, after it has been placed 
allowing the TrackSections to use another conguration, which e.g. could be
one the four dierent congurations available for a point section.
Any AnchorConnectionPoint, that is used by an Anchor must be connected
internally to a VisualSection. If this is not the case, an error has occurred, and
the TrackSection can be deemed incorrect. The reason for this requirement, is
that other TrackSection are connected to the section through the Anchors. So
if a VisualSection was not attached to an Anchor, it would give a situation in
which the network track layout would be disconnected, and it could potentially
cause a derailing, as a train would not have a track to continue on.
==PAGE:110==
96
Design
6.3.5.1
Anchor Connection Point
As seen in Figure 6.7, an AnchorConnectionPoint is placed on the border that
outlines a TrackSection.
To achieve this, an AnchorConnectionPoint, has
as seen in the domain model of Figure 6.12, a distance and a PointLocation
associated with it.
The PointLocation denes at which side of the TrackSections border, the
AnchorConnectionPoint will be located, given as EAST, NORTH, WEST and
NORTH. Which side the AnchorConnectionPoint is positioned on, determines
from which corner the distance will be calculated.

Figure 6.13: Position of AnchorConnectionPoint, determined from side and
