How the exact position is calculated, is shown in Figure 6.13, which depicts
which corner the positions is calculated from when placed at a given side. The
provided distance, decides a percentages of the given side, that it should be
placed at. This percentage is therefore either the height or width of the Track-
Section, depending on the side it is located at.
6.3.6
Figures
The Figures is a crucial part of the network, as they are responsible for show-
ing everything in the network beside the TrackSections.
This means they
are responsible for providing the graphical appearance of the markerboards,
level-crossings etc. In order for them to be able to do this, a great level of
customizability must be acquired. A way this customization, can be achieved is
by using the domain model, shown in Figure 6.14.

The idea behind the Figure, is that it is built up by parts, each describing a
specic shape of the Figure. Each part will have a background colour assigned,
which may be changed during run-time, to simulate state changes.
Currently there are two types of FiguresParts, the CircleFigurePart and the
PointFigurePart, each enables one to dene dierent shapes.
PointFigurePart Will contain several PercentageLocations that describes
the points that the FiguresParts should be constructed of. This may be
a single line, in case closeFigure is set to false, or any type of shape if
this is set to true, as it will then make an edge between the rst and last
dened PercentageLocation (and ll the shape with the dened colour).
CircleFigurePart Is used to dene a circle, or parts of one, to be used as a
part in a Figure. This is done, as shown in Figure 6.15, with the use of
a starting angle (as degrees, where 0◦is located directly to the right of
the centre). The spanning angle, then determines how many angles from
the starting angle the circle should be drawn (and it will be drawn in a
counter-clockwise fashion).
With this approach, one can dene a full circle, by having the starting
angle at 0◦and a spanning angle of 360◦. However the size of the circle
is dependent on the radius  given as a double. Finally the centre of the
gure is determine by a single PercentageLocation.
==PAGE:112==

Starting 
Angle
Spanning Angle
Figure 6.15: Utilization of the variables for constructing a CircleFigurePart
In order to facilitate which gure part will be placed on top of each other 
layers are used, to determine which part is to be placed on top of the other. A
lower number means that it will be placed further behind when drawn. This
means the higher the layer, the better the chances are of seeing the part.
Example
Lets take an example of how a Figure might be created, given the
visual representation of markerboards, as described in Section 4.2.2. And in
order to construct the markerboard as a Figure, we need two FiguresParts,
as shown in Figure 6.16.

arrow, is being used to construct the visual representation of the markerboard.
In this case both FiguresParts will be a PointFigurePart, where both has
closed set to true  as they are showing their respective background colours.
The purpose of the given gure, is to be placed within an Extension that denes
the size of the area that needs to be lled.
However when the two FiguresParts are combined to a single gure, we have
that the arrow will be placed on top of the square, due to the arrow having a
larger value for the layer.

In order to support the dierent features needed by the environment, several
types of extensions has been established, as seen in the domain model of
Figure 6.17.

Each extension has some information that it must contain, such as its precise lo-
cation in the network, as well as its dimension (given as width and height). These
are stored within a LocationInfo, that will be included with each Extension,
and is utilized by the Figures to ensure that they will be drawn at the correct
place of the canvas (i.e. the network-diagram).
The idea is that each Extension, must have an area in which its contained
Figure may operate within. This is where the percentages for the given Per-
centageLocations, described in Section 6.3.6 comes into play, as it will use
these percentage to place itself within the area dened by the Extension.
This means that an Extension must have some xed point that it will be
located at. This is however the job of each individual sub-class to provide, as
they are most likely to have dierent approaches on how to handle the location
==PAGE:114==
100
Design
of the Extension.
Currently two approaches are available on how one controls where the Extension
is located, and how to centre the area the Extension creates.
FigureExtension One approach is to utilize an FigureExtensionArea, as de-
scribed in Section 6.3.4. If a Figure is placed within an FigureExtension
 containing an FigureExtensionArea, the Figure will be drawn from the
left-most FigureExtensionPoint, that the area is constructed o.
As a VisualSection may be angled  the width and height of the extension
is given by the rotated areas, such that all corners of the rotated area is
contained within an rectangle.
This can be done, by constructing an rectangle containing the two left
corners of the area, and a rectangle containing the right corner of the
area. If one then takes the union between these, we will have the area
that contains the rotated area. However as we only have two of the four
points dened  we will need to nd the last two points. This can be done
by rotating the points to a neutral position (where the VisualSection is
horizontal).
θ
Figure 6.18: Calculation of the four corner of the FigureExtensionArea when
rotated
From Figure 6.18, we can see how the calculation is done, as we will rotate
the two known points by the θ-angle (calculated as we know every side of
the right-angled triangle the may be constructed between the two points
of the VisualSection). When rotated we can simply get the remaining
two corners of the area. After this, the point will be rotated back with θ
as the angle. This provides us with the original area with all four corners
known, and we can with ease nd the surrounding Extension, by utilizing
the methods described earlier.
The constructed area, that the Extension will cover is then shown in
Figure 6.19, which is needed for the area to be drawn fully. This is due to
==PAGE:115==
6.3 Domain Model
101
Figure 6.19: Constructed area for the extension, shown with dashed lines
the use of EMF/GMF, that only allows a component to be drawn within
its borders (and as GMF can't handle rotated boxes, this is the solution
one needs to apply).
When a Figure is to be drawn within an FigureExtension, the points
will be handled in the same fashion, as when the boundary was calculated.
This means that each point will be calculated when the FigureExtension
is in the neutral position, and rotated back to their actual position. This
provides an easy way to determine the look of the Figure.
LocationExtension Using LocationExtensions instead of the FigureExten-
sion, enables a Figure to be placed at a specic location on either a
VisualSection or TrackSection  depending of the type of Location-
Extension that is used.
Given that it is placed at a specic location, with a width and height,
we will need to decide how such an extension is placed around the given
location. For this purpose one may utilize a LocationPlace, that decides
where the location is placed with respect to the Extension. There are
currently ve dierent values, as seen in Figure 6.10. For instance if the
extension has the value UpperLeft for its location, it would have the
FixedExtendableLocation or ExtendableLocation placed at the upper
left corner of the Extension (and the Figure would calculate its position
from the upper left corner of the Extension).
With this said, we have that there exist two sub-types of the Location-
Extension, depending on which placement the extension should have.
GeneralLocationExtension This is when a user wants to have an Ex-
tension located somewhere on the node that represent the Track-
Section.
This is done by utilizing a FixedExtendableLocation,
that is described in Section 6.3.4.
VisualLocationExtension Another approach is to have the Extension
hooked onto a VisualSection, which could be used for the marker-
==PAGE:116==
102
Design
boards.
In this case the Extension will utilize an Extendable-
Location, see Section 6.3.4.
6.3.7.1
Share Attributes
One special feature about the VisualLocationExtension is the possibility for
it to share its properties with other VisualLocationExtensions.
In order for it to be shared, the variable shareAttributes, must be set to true.
If this is the case, two other conditions has to be fullled in order for another
VisualLocationExtension to use an existing Extensions properties.
1. The ID of the Extension must match the existing Extensions ID. If two
elements ID are equal to each other, the process of checking whether they
may share attributes is started.
2. The type of the Extensions must be equal to each other  this means that
a level-crossing may only share properties with other level-crossings.
This is done to have a consistent image of what properties a certain ID
has available, as dierent Extensions properties are not stored under a
single ID.
If both these requirements are fullled, the two Extensions may share their
properties, and they may be considered to be a single Extension.
6.3.8
States
In order to incorporate dynamic features that highlight dierent states for each
object in the network, one has to determine how each unique state for an object
will be visualized. A domain model for supporting this dynamic feature is shown
in Figure 6.20.
The idea is that each state will have a Trigger assigned, which acts as a check
for when a state can be considered active. The trigger has several properties as-
signed, which states what value each property will have  and in case all assigned
properties for the state is the same as the current values for the properties, the
state may be deemed as active.
The idea of having children for the states, is such that a user may use a state
as a guard for several states  in which they all require the triggers on their
==PAGE:117==
6.3 Domain Model
103
«Reference»
AnchorState
Trigger
ID: String
+canTrigger(Network): boolean
«Reference»
TrackSectionState
«Reference»
FigureState
Property
value: String
State
*
children
1
property: string
1
Figure 6.20: Domain model for states
parent state to be true. This means that the hierarchy of states act as an AND
barrier, such that all ancestors of a state must be active before itself can be
active.
The Triggers themselves may span several values, so they act as an OR state-
ment, in which only a singular value of the ones stated, has to be true in order
for the Trigger being able to trigger. Due to the restriction, that the proper-
ties of a Trigger are all stored as string, we must utilize a unique character to
separate the values stored within the properties for the Trigger. This unique
character has been chosen to be  | . So in case we had a trigger containing
the value "m1|m2" as one of its strings, it would mean that the value for the
property would either have to be m1 or m2 for the Trigger to be valid.
The state that will be executed when a Trigger is active, depends on the
concrete type of state, and we currently have three concrete types, that inherits
from the State. These States are the FigureStates, TrackSectionStates
and AnchorStates.
6.3.8.1
Figure State
The various Figures, utilized throughout a network, such as markerboards may
have dynamic properties  in the case of the markerboards, a colour will have
to change according to its state. For this reason we have FigureStates that
describes what will change for a given FigurePart of the overall gure  when
the properties are changed. The FigureState class can be seen in Figure 6.21.
The idea behind the FigureState, is that a certain state may alter any visual
representation of any FigurePart (with the ID used for reference) contained in
the Figure that uses the FigureState. This may be the outline of the part or
simply the colour of it. In case of the markerboard, it would have two states,
in which one will have a green colour, whilst the other would have red set as its

colour. The outline of the part will not change  and therefore no points will

the part will not change.
We have two concrete FigurePartState that a FigureState may contain, one
for adjusting the CircleFigureParts and one for the PointFigureParts.

alter the colour of the FigurePart, and they will only be generated in case a
change occurs for the referenced FigurePart. The reference is done by utilizing
the ID of the FigureParts, of any given Figure.

dened. It is likewise possible to either hide or show the given section  by
utilizing the visible variable.

6.4
Interlocking Table
The interlocking table, is what contains all routes that can be supported by the
related network, and a domain model for an interlocking table can be seen in
Figure 6.25.
An interlocking table, or RouteTable has a reference to the Network that it
is related to. This enables it to save its contained routes to the network, as
ExternalObjects, done by utilizing the saveToNetwork() method.
The interlocking table, is to be constructed as an static object, allowing any
plug-in to retrieve the RouteTable, if needed. One can extract/create a route-
table, by calling getInstance(), which will retrieve the current instantiated in-
terlocking table, or create a new one in case it has not been instantiated.
Otherwise we have that the RouteTable will contain all routes, related to the
given Network  which will contain references to objects as described by the
DSL, see Section 2.1.1.
Since there only exist two dierent objects, that can be referenced, namely
TrackSections and Extensions, we will solve the issue of knowing which ob-
jects may be referenced, by stating which types may be put into a given rela-
tion. This means that the signals reference to the Extension, may only contain
Extensions of type markerboard. By having this restriction, when trying to
add to a given collection, we ensure that no illegal objects are referenced in a
route.

6.5
XML Conguration
As stated in Section 6.1, we choose to utilize XML to support extendibility. In
order for an environment to work with XML, it is best to dene a denition
that must be followed when writing the XML. For this purpose one could either
choose DTD or XML Schema. In our case we choose the schema approach, as
it provides a more object oriented approach for dening what is a legal cong-
uration.
When using the XML schemas, we will utilize the fact that it can be used
to verify the correctness of a given XML document, to see if it comply with
the schema. This means that we will not have to check each entry point of a
conguration le is correct  but we rather assume it is correct in case it passes
the verication in relation to the schema.
==PAGE:123==
6.5 XML Conguration
109
The XML Schema top-level element is a conguration, that denes which parts
should be included. In most cases the XML Schema just denes an approach to
dene the objects dened in Section 6.3  as these are the element that need to
be instantiated from the XML conguration.
6.5.1
Conguration
The conguration is needed to dene the elements that needs to be added to
the environment, i.e. all the TrackSection, Extension and Figures. Each of
these types of objects all have a unique representation within the XML Schema,
that allow one to dene the dierent customizable aspects of the object.

and gures are optional.

Several ConnectionPoints are dened, as Anchor Point and Internal Point in
the schema. These elements allow one to create AnchorConnectionPoint and
InternalConnectionPoint respectively  with all the features required to place
the given point within the track section.

etc. within the track section that contains the states. And the Trigger for the

the moment the track section is created  see Section 6.5.6 for more details.

lastly, the XML element is what denes how the type of track section must
be serialized to XML, when the model checker is called  see more details in
Section 6.5.8.

An important entry for any of the elements is the XML Element, as seen in

The will dene a hierarchy in which the given element will be serialized to
XML. Each object that needs to be saved has an Element as the top-root,
dening the name for the Element, and it may contain several other Elements
as children. Each Element may then contain several Attributes, where the name
is the attributes name, and the value is the value of property that has the name,
that matches the provided value.
This allows an object to be serialized to XML, and allows for any possible XML
structure, where there is a single root element.
The complete XML Schema dened can be found in Appendix D
6.6

Having the XML Conguration stated in Section 6.5, enables us to create new
types of extensions, sections etc. by altering a XML le. However in order to
utilize this customizability, we will need to design a component to handle the
XML and create objects from the provided conguration.
This is where the conguration sub-component in Figure 6.1 is used.
The
domain-model for this component can be seen in Figure 6.34.

Figure 6.34: Domain model for the Conguration sub-component
The idea behind the conguration sub-component, is that we have a Configu-
ration, which can be retrieved statically by calling getInstance(). This means
==PAGE:130==
116
Design
that we at all times will have a single conguration loaded  which any other
component may call to create objects, retrieve details about the XML congu-
ration etc.
All information contained within the XML conguration le will be stored in
a ConfigurationXMLHandler, in case the provided XML le corresponds to
the provided XML schema.
The ConfigurationXMLHandler is a static in-
stance, that is initialized, whenever someone calls getXMLHandler() on the
Configuration, as seen in Figure 6.35. This trigger the process of loading the
conguration le, which is done by storing all XML elements of the congura-
tion le in a map for their respective class, so TrackSections are stored in one
map, Extension in another map etc. In each map, the type-attribute of the
individual element, will be used as a key for the individual XML element.
This will allow for easy retrieval of the XML data, that is loaded from the
conguration le  as the XML elements are stored as is, and may be utilized at
a later time. The ConfigurationXMLHandler is handling the XML data, and
make it easy for later retrieval, which is achieved by having the maps for each
object type.
6.6.1
Factories
Having loaded all data from the conguration le, the environment is able to
create the dened types. However one cannot utilize the already existing meth-
ods, that handles the creation of the domain objects, out-of-box when using
EMF/GMF, as these will simply create an empty shell for a given object. As
we will need to provide the conguration, stated in the XML le, we will need
to congure each object in our own way.
We have therefore chosen to move all creation of objects into the conguration-
subcomponent, that will utilize factory methods for the creation of objects.
Currently there exist four factories, one for each type of element that needs to
be created, as seen in Figure 6.34.
SectionFactory is responsible for creating track sections of the given type,
and conguring these according to the XML element for a section of the
provided type. The XML element, may contain the information given in
Section 6.5.2, which needs to be utilized in the creation.
ExtensionFactory can create new extensions of a type  this is including the
==PAGE:131==
6.6 Conguration
117
intended gure, that must be placed within the extension. The information
described in Section 6.5.3 will be utilized for this purpose.
FigureFactory creates the gures to be used in any type of extension. It uses
the information stated in 6.5.4 for the creation of a gure.
LabelFactory provides a method for creating and placing a label at the correct
place for a property.
All factories will utilize the ConfigurationXMLHandler to retrieve the needed
XML element, i.e. explanation of what a given type must include. The factories
will then extract the needed information from the XML element, and instantiate
a correct object of the provided type.
The reason for having these factories, is that it gathers the creation of object at
a central location of the program, and will ease maintenance of the environment
at later times. In case a change for a specic creation has to be introduced, one
will simply have to change one of the factory methods.
The Configuration in it self, will then act as an container for all the factories,
and will contain methods that directly call their respective counter-part in a
factory. This is ensured by the interface IConguration, which must enable the
same methods, as the implemented factories has dened.

Chapter 7
Implementation
When implementing the environment in EMF/GMF, there are many decisions
that one has to take into account when implementing the environment with
these framework. In this chapter, we will make a run-through of some of the
implementation details that has been made. Most of these are related to details
about the use of EMF/GMF, however general decisions about the implementa-
tion will also be provided. This is to give a quick overview of what has been
implemented, and how it has been implemented.
7.1
Adapters
Adapters, are an important feature in EMF/GMF, as they allow objects to be
notied about changes from any given EObject. One would then be able to
listen for changes in a given EObject, and trigger an eect that is related to
this change.
The adapters has been utilized throughout the implementation for various pur-
poses, such as keeping track of the location of sections, or more importantly
listening to changes in the properties, such that we may trigger any states that
has become active due to the changed properties.
==PAGE:134==
120
Implementation
7.1.1
Property Adapter
A notable adapter that has been implemented, is the one detecting whether the
value for any property has changed. In order to not get a notication each time
a single property has its value changed (which would trigger a lot of notications
at once, as the property values will be switched at the same time, when running
the simulation steps).
We would instead, utilize that every property for an
object, is contained within the same object (as a map).
One could then register an adapter to this object, and have it contain a variable,
that could have its value changed, when a notications should be send out (to
notify about changes in the properties). This would mean, that we would receive
a single notication instead of one for each property that has been updated.
When a notication is received, we would then realise that some properties has
changed their values, and we would be able to compare them to the states, to
see if any of them may trigger. And in case some trigger, apply the states of
the object, that contains the given states.
Using adapters for the properties, is however just one of the many uses of
adapters in the implementation, as they are used in various placed to synchro-
nize objects with each other. Any object that relies on a variable on another
object, must install an adapter of the given object, such that notications are
received when the object changes.
7.2
Tools
When using GMF as the framework, there are several tools, that enables the
user to do, what he intends with the editor. Any action performed in relation
to the editor, is handled by tools. This could be the creation of new elements,
selecting an existing element etc. The tasks that the tools must be able to solve
are many, which is why one can have equally many tools, that denes what
work-ow to use in any given situation.
We have implemented/extended several tools, to alter the work-ow for the
existing tools, such that we get the work-ow that is intended (as described in
Chapter 5).
==PAGE:135==
7.2 Tools
121
7.2.1
Selection Tool
The selection of objects in a standard GMF editor, works by simply selecting
the last created object, that is present at a given position. This will not work in
our case, as one may have extensions that overlaps that boundaries of an anchor
(thereby disabling the selection of the anchor).
This means that we need a priority on which elements are to be selected in the
editor, when multiple elements overlap each other. The way it is achieved is
by retrieving all EditParts, that are present at the given position (i.e. where
the user clicked). All EditParts may be retrieved through an EditPartRegistry,
stored within the current viewer.
With all the EditParts, we can extract the gure from each of them and see
whether the position, is within their boundaries. The EditParts that are lo-
cated at the given position will then be sorted according to which type they
are.
1. Anchors are most important, and must be selected if one is located at the
provided position. This is so one, at all times may alter the conguration
that a section has, by adjusting the location of an Anchor.
2. Labels are less important, as they allow one to adjust properties. So in
case no Anchors are present at the given position, a Label would be next
in line for selection.
3. In case no Anchors or Labels are present at the given position, but we
have Extensions, these will be chosen next. This is as they do not take
up as much space as TrackSections, and in most cases they lie on-top of
a TrackSection.
4. Lastly, we have the TrackSections, which will only be selected in case no
other elements are is present at the provided position, at which the user
has clicked.
When every element at the given position has been sorted, according to the
description above, we will simply take the rst element in the sorted list, as the
selection. This means that in case multiple elements of the same type is present,
it is random which one will be selected. However it gives a clearer denition
on which element will get selected, when it is no longer the last placed element
that will get selected.
==PAGE:136==
122
Implementation
7.2.2
Connection Tool
The default tool, used to create connections between the dierent Anchors has
the same issue as with the selection tool. It simply tries to match the latest
created element at a given position to be an Anchor  and if this is not the case
it will not enable one to create the connection.
To solve this, we use the same approach as for the selection tool, however we
only add the Anchors that might be at the position. This means that we will
just select one of the Anchors at the given position (if there are any) and enable
one to create a connection.
7.2.2.1
Connection Creation
The tool will when possible try to get a creation command for the given object,
that needs to be created  in this case a Connection. This command decides
if the tool should enable the creation of the Connection. Out-of-the-box, there
are no limitation on what it may be created between, as long as the two objects
are of the supported type (in our case Anchors).
We have added some restrictions on what Anchors, a connection may be estab-
lished between. This is solely based on the placement of the Anchors, meaning
that an Anchor placed on the west side, may only be connected with Anchors
placed on the east side. In short, it means that a connection may only be es-
tablished between two Anchors placed on opposite sides. This means that one
cannot create an invalid network  in which two sections would make a U-turn
between each other.
7.2.3
Creation Tools
The standard tools that is used for creation, functions as we would expect.
However we will need to adjust them to function with our custom creation of
objects, using the factories described in Section 6.6.1.
For this reason, we will need to extend what data to be send along, when a
creation request is made by the tool, in order to call the creation command
related to the given object type.
This extra data can be inserted by utilizing that a request, may be extended
with extra data, by using the map, extended data  relating a key-word to any
==PAGE:137==
7.3 Track Sections
123
object (we will only insert strings). The request will then have its extended data
lled with the needed data, that will be utilized in the creation of an object.
ToolEntry
ExtensionToolEntry
InterlockingToolEntry
Figure 7.1: Creation tools implemented
As seen in Figure 7.1, we have two tool-denitions. A general (Interlocking-
ToolEntry) and a specialized (ExtensionToolEntry) tool, where the specialized
deals with the creation of extensions.
The general tool, will simply append
which type that the created object should have. This needs to be used by all
tools, as the factories will need it, in the creation of objects (i.e. to retrieve the
correct XML element).
Beside the type, the tool entries will have their extended data, lled out with
other needed information, such that they can instantiate the objects correctly.
7.3
Track Sections
When a track section is to be constructed visually in GMF, it will be created
as a top-node in the diagram. This means that it will be placed directly in
the network-diagram, and its position is given by the placement it has in the
diagram representing the network.
A problem that occurred when constructing the TrackSections graphically def-
inition, was that it should both contain elements (InternalConnectionPoints
and AnchorConnectionPoint) within its boundaries, and also have Anchors
placed on its boundary.
An issue that occurred, when a TrackSection was
dened as a single rectangle, was that the Anchors were not functioning as
intended, as they could not be placed correctly.
A solution for this problem, can be seen in Figure 7.2, in which a TrackSection
will consist of two components.
==PAGE:138==
124
Implementation
Track Section
Track Section Comparment
Anchors
Figure 7.2: How a TrackSection is structured graphically
One of these components is a compartment (Track Section Compartment),
that will be placed within the boundaries of the TrackSection  and ll the
TrackSection completely.
This compartment will contain the Connection-
Points related to the TrackSection.
Having this compartment allows for
Anchors to be placed with ease on the boundary of the TrackSection  as
an axed edit part. And both the ConnectionPoint and Anchors behaves as
intended, thereby eliminating the issue.
Since the compartment, will have the same size as the TrackSection it is con-
tained within, the solution will therefore work as if every object in the compart-
ment, were to be placed directly in the TrackSection. And each Connection-
Point, will be placed according to their denition, as stated in Section 6.3.3.1
and 6.3.5.1. The ConnectionPoints update their location, whenever a noti-
cation is received from the parent, i.e. the TrackSection. When received,
each ConnectionPoint will update their location according to their provided
denition  by using the position and dimension of the TrackSection.
7.3.1
Visual Section
The VisualSections will be placed as an connection in the network-diagram,
making them a top-level element. For this reason it would be possible to se-
lect the VisualSections, when clicking on them, however this should not be
possible, as they just represent a visual aspect of the TrackSections.
==PAGE:139==
7.3 Track Sections
125
GMF has support for easy disabling of certain entities, which can be done by
simply overriding the method isSelectable() and return false at all times.
This disables the possibility of selecting the element  and ensures that one se-
lects the underlying TrackSection, when one tries to select the VisualSection
(or other elements in case they are at the position).
The placement of the VisualSection is ensured by being hooked onto the two
end-points, given as ConnectionPoints. Due to this relation, the VisualSec-
tion, will always update its placement, when the position of the Connection-
Points are updated, either when the TrackSection is moved or resized.
7.3.2
Anchors
The Anchor are as stated placed on the boundaries of the TrackSection as
axed edit parts. This simply mean, that they are attached to the part that
is dened by the TrackSection, and are moved whenever the TrackSection is
relocated, or resized.
However to gure out at which locations, an Anchor, may be placed at, we will
need to use a BorderItemLocator.
The BorderItemLocator that is used as a
standard, simply enables one to place the given axed edit part anywhere on
the side of the edit part, that it has been dened to be attached to (SOUTH,
WEST, NORTH or EAST), or simply be able to place it anywhere on the border.
This will not work for our Anchors, as it was decided that they would have
specic locations, at which they may be located, see Section 6.3.5.
Placement
So in order to place the Anchor, a new BorderItemLocator has
been implemented. This will override the method getValidLocation(propro-
sedLocation), since it is called whenever a valid location for the axed edit
part needs to be found.
The new method, will simply take all valid locations, i.e. AnchorConnection-
Points, that the selected Anchor may be placed at. And then nd the closest
of these according to the proposed location (i.e. the provided argument). The
closest AnchorConnectionPoints will then be stored, as the finalLocation,
such that it can be retrieved at a later time.
The super-method will be called, such that an arbitrary location can be returned
(as it will be used for feedback purposes, i.e.
the returned location will be
highlighted on the border of the TrackSection).
==PAGE:140==
126
Implementation
However in order to fully incorporate the new BorderItemLocator, a new se-
lection policy, must be implemented. This will change what command will be
constructed, when it is decided that the Anchor has to be moved to a new loca-
tion. When moved, the getMoveCommand(request) from the installed selection
policy will get called  which is then responsible for moving the Anchor to the
new location through a generated command. Figure 7.3, shows that process of
constructing the command when an Anchor is moved.

old AnchorConnectPoint
Update the PropertyValue for the position 
of the anchor in the properties for the 
TrackSection

update the properties accordingly to these

new AnchorConnectPoint

being the location for the Anchor

current valid location loaded and 
show as feedback
Figure 7.3: Process of moving an anchor
Each of the steps, shown in the activity diagram of Figure 7.3, will utilize
Commands when modifying the objects, and each of these Commands will be ap-
==PAGE:141==
7.4 Extension
127
pended to a CompoundCommand. The CompoundCommand, ensures that all gener-
ated Commands will be executed in one step. This guarantees that we will be
able to undo/redo the commands in one step when executed on the command
stack.
We ensure that we utilize our own selection policy, by changing which layout
edit policy that is returned when createChildEditPolicy(child) is called on
the TrackSection. Here our specialized selection policy is returned whenever
an Anchor is provided as a child.
7.4
Extension
The Extensions are what is used to apply extra features to a particular track-
or visual-section. The placement of such an Extension, is given by listening
for when the TrackSection that the extension is attached to, changes either its
Location or Dimension.
We ensure that the Extension receives these notication, by having an Adapter
installed on both the Location and Dimension. By having these Adapters, we
may receive an notication, whenever a variable in the object that we listens to
changes. This is used to alter the location, the Extension has, as it will check
whether it will need to update its position, whenever an appropriate notication
is received, i.e. from either the Dimension or the Location.
In general, when an Extension gets a notication, we will ensure that the
Extension is placed correctly. However, as the position one receives by calling
the LocationExtension is relative to the coordinates within the TrackSection,
meaning one would have to adjust the coordinates in relation to the top-left cor-
ner of the TrackSection borders (i.e. the location of the TrackSection). And
depending on how the extension should be placed, according to its Location-
Place, the top-left corner of the Extension is determined (by using the Loca-
tionPlace and the provided location).
With the external denition of the Extension solved, one would still need a way
of highlighting the Figure stored within the Extension. This is handled in a
very simple fashion, as it will be the Figures own task to draw what is needed.
This is done by calling the Figure to draw itself, whenever the Extension has
to be update its graphical appearance.
==PAGE:142==
128
Implementation
7.4.1
Figures
Whenever the Extension that a given Figure is stored within needs to be
updated visually, it will call the Figure to ll out the Extension.
A Figure will draw each FigurePart according to their denition, using Per-
centagesLocations, closed etc.
All the FigureParts, has been sorted accordingly in relation to the layer, they
are to be placed in. And the drawing of the Figures will start with the Figure-
Parts with the smallest layer (as later drawn FigureParts will be drawn on
top of earlier drawn parts). Each FigurePart, may have a single active state
that it must comply with  which is found before any painting begins.
When all active states have been extracted, one may apply them to the Figure-
Parts, that will draw the outline, dened by the PercentageLocations. This
could be a closed gure, with a given background colour (as stated in an active
state or by default). Each part will then draw itself within the Extension  as all
locations are translated to be located within the boundaries of the Extension,
or if they are outside the boundaries, nothing will be shown.
Colouring
The colouring of the FigureParts, would as a standard be com-
pletely non-transparent.
However as this would cover up everything that is
placed behind a given Figure, it was decided to include some transparency on
all colours applied. The only colour to be handled dierently is white (with
RGB = (0,0,0)), which will be granted full-transparency.
7.5
Labels
The labels contained within a network, needs to be constructed such that it can
be placed anywhere within the network-layout. For this reason it will need to
have a separate top-node dened to be placed within the network diagram.
This top-node will be represented by an invisible rectangle (such that it cannot
be seen when a label is created). The label itself will however not be placed in
the node, since it would simply be contained within the boundaries of the node,
and as we want the node to be non-selectable it would at the same time hinder
one from altering the textual information of the label (and ultimately the value
of the property).
==PAGE:143==
7.6 Load Selected Network
129
LABEL
Label Container
Figure 7.4: Graphical component related to a label
A solution for this is shown in Figure 7.4, as seen, we have the top-node 
represented by the Label Container. The label, that will contain the textural
information to be edited, will be constructed as a an axed external edit part.
As it is axed it will be xed to the border of the Label Container  which means
that the Label Container, ultimately will dene the location of the label.
The size of the invisible Label Container, must be as minimal as possible, i.e.
it will have a desired dimension of (1,1)  indicating a width and height of one.
This ensures that our label will be placed at the position dened for the Label
Container, as it is placed directly on the border of the Label Container.
7.6
Load Selected Network
Due to the design choice to have external plug-ins utilize a given network, we
must ensure that they are able to detect when a network-diagram is opened, or
which network is shown in the workbench.
In order to solve this, an extra extension point will be created for the editor,
given as an interface ILoadHandler, see in Figure 7.5. Each component that
needs to extract information from the current network, must therefore extend
this interface  and register it as an extension for the extension point. The
interface it self, will contain a single methods, that passes the opened network
as an argument.
«interface»
ILoadHandler
load(network)
Figure 7.5: Model for the ILoadHandler
==PAGE:144==
130
Implementation
The way the extension point is implemented, is by installing a PartListener,
onto the workbench page, that represents the given workbench window. The
page, controls all parts contained in Eclipse (diagrams, properties etc.), and a
PartListener is able to detect when a given workbench part is activated (which
could be the editor, containing the network).
Whenever a part is activated, we will load the network of the currently active
diagram (if one is opened), and use it as the argument for the extension point.
Each plug-in, that then extends the extension point will be called, meaning the
currently opened network will be distributed to every plug-in that needs it. Each
plug-in is then responsible for handling the loading of the network in their own
way, e.g. the route-view will load the routes stored within the network (which
will be stored as an ExternalObject).
So in case any plug-in has stored any data within the network, as an External-
Object, it can load it when a network is opened.
7.7
Running the Existing Tool
All communication to the external tool-chain, was as shown in Section 6.2,
handled by the simulation-component.
However the exact communication is
done by utilizing functionality in Java, that enables one to execute external
programs, which in our case is done with Runtime.getRuntime().exec().
This enables us to execute a make-le, that will run the tool for us, and generate
the needed les (containing counter-examples, static-errors etc.). The make-le
to be used has a set of options that can be altered to achieve dierent results,
such as whether to use manually created routes or if they should be generated,
number of steps that the verication should take etc.
The content of the make-le depends solely on what task should be performed.
And we have two general tasks that may be performed, namely verication and
generation of routes.
7.7.1
Verication
When deciding one decides to verify an interlocking-specication, both for se-
mantically errors, and to see whether any safety property are violated. In order
for one to execute this, the process shown in Figure 7.6 is to be used. This pro-
==PAGE:145==
7.7 Running the Existing Tool
131
cess is quite straight-forward, since we will rst create the XML le, describing
the interlocking-specication  this is done with the XMLHandler, as described
in Section 6.2.3.
After the XML le has been correctly created, we shall create the make-le that
will run the tool-chain. It is currently the tool-chains job, to check whether
any violations has been established in the network or route-table. If any errors,
happens during the execution, i.e.
the network is missing something, or it
violates a static rule  these errors will be loaded and displayed for the user,
such that the errors can be corrected.
However, in case no errors occur, we can load the results that has been generated
by the tool-chain. In some cases, no results will be loaded, as the verication was
a success. However, if an error in the verication has occurred, i.e. a counter-
example has been generated, we load the provided counter-example into the
environment.
Simultaneous, routes will be loaded if this is needed, and the
results for the safety-properties will be loaded and shown as well.
These three processes will occur concurrently, which means that we will start
three threads, each handling one of the concurrent tasks.
7.7.2
Generate Routes
When one wants to generate routes  the process shown in Figure 7.6 has to be
made. The make-le generate will simply set that, we will not use our own routes
(-genRouteTab will not be used in the make-le), which trigger the tool-chain
to generate every possible route, that can occur in the provided network.
Moreover, we will state in the make-le, that the verication will not have to
run for any steps. This simply ensures that we will only generate the routes, and
not actually check whether the interlocking-system is sound (which would be a
waste of time, as the user is only interested in getting the routes generated).

Results
In this chapter, we will highlight the state of the environment, and which features
has been implemented. Moreover we will show an example on how to extend the
environment with new types  to illustrate the exibility of the environment.
Furthermore known issues and causes for these will be highlighted, to illustrate
that the environment is not fully implemented, and some issues are still present.
8.1
Implemented Environment
The environment that has been developed, support all the functional and non-
functional requirements listed in Section 2.4. This has been achieved by utilizing
Eclipse as the environment to be developed for, and EMF and GMF used as the
developing framework.
This gave us an easy to extend solution, that provided much of the needed func-
tionality out-of-the-box. Figure 8.1 shows an example of the nal environment,
with an already constructed network-layout (most of it is not shown, as the
network extends outside the view-port).
The palette shown to the right in Figure 8.1, is the tools, that is used to create
the dierent objects within the network-diagram. The network-diagram is what
==PAGE:148==

Results
Figure 8.1: Final Environment loaded with the conguration le in Ap-
pendix E
contains all sections and extensions, and we can in the currently opened network,
see that it at least contains two point- and three linear-sections. However this
is only a small sub-set of the total network.
Otherwise we have that the interlocking-specication, can be controlled with
several windows, placed in the bottom of Figure 8.1. These control the sim-
ulation/verication, the routes and all actions related to the labels (such as
creation, and movement).
8.1.1
Executing Verication
If we try to run the verication on the interlocking-specication given in Fig-

8.2
Adding new Element
The environment is easy to extend with both new sections and extensions. This
is due to the XML conguration, that denes every element that may be con-
tained within a network-layout.
As an example, we can try and add the level-crossing to the already estab-
lished conguration, which can be seen in Appendix E. In order to extend this,
we will need to rst dene a Figure, that will represent a level-crossing. For
this, we will need a denition on how the level-crossing will look when deployed,
and a suggestion for this appearance can be seen in Figure 8.6. This appearance
is not related to the domain, but is only created for demonstration purposes.
Level-Crossing
Figure 8.6: Proposed graphical appearance of a level-crossing
This simply represents the two barriers that may be next to the track sec-
tion, where a level-crossing is occurring.
In order to incorporate such a
level-crossing in the environment, one has to go through two steps:
Dene a Figure A Figure will have to be dened, that will be used as the
graphical denition for an Extension.
Dene the Extension The Extension, that must contain the gure, has to
==PAGE:152==
138
Results
be specied  i.e. the width and height of the extension, if it may be
shared etc.
But if we rst look into how one would dene the Figure, we will see that the
graphical representation shown in Figure 8.6, consists of two parts, namely an
upper- and lower-barrier. These can be represented by two PointParts, one for
each barrier  where both are not set to be closed (meaning they will appear as
a set of lines).
Since we assume that the Extension that will contain the Figure is to be
anchored to the center of the Extension  the gure will be drawn from the
top-left corner of the Extension. For this reason, we can with ease dene the
points to be used by the PointParts.
Assuming the section that we are attached to, will be at 50.0% vertical, we can
use this as a reference point. So this means we get the following two PointParts.
• One part consisting of the points, (0.0, 100.0), (100.0, 100.0) and (100.0,
50.0).
• and another part, consisting of the points, (0.0, 50.0), (0.0, 0.0) and (100.0,
0.0).
This will then be inserted into an XML description, as seen in Listing 8.1, that
follows the XML schema for the conguration.
Listing 8.1: Figure for a level-crossing
<config : Figure
type=" level −crossing ">
<f i g u r e s : PointPart
closed=" f a l s e ">
<f i g u r e s : CommonPart ID="top−crossing "
Layer="0"></ f i g u r e s : CommonPart>
<f i g u r e s : Point x=" 0.0 " y=" 100.0 " />
<f i g u r e s : Point x=" 100.0 " y=" 100.0 " />
<f i g u r e s : Point x=" 100.0 " y=" 50.0 " />
</ f i g u r e s : PointPart>
<f i g u r e s : PointPart
closed=" f a l s e ">
<f i g u r e s : CommonPart ID="bottom−crossing "
Layer="0"></ f i g u r e s : CommonPart>
<f i g u r e s : Point x=" 0.0 " y=" 50.0 " />
<f i g u r e s : Point x=" 0.0 " y=" 0.0 " />
<f i g u r e s : Point x=" 100.0 " y=" 0.0 " />
==PAGE:153==
8.2 Adding new Element
139
</ f i g u r e s : PointPart>
</ config : Figure>
The XML description in Listing 8.1, only provides the graphical representation
of a level-crossing. How it is to be placed is dependent on the Extension,
that is to contain the description of the Figure.
The Extension will provide the dimensions that the Figure will utilize, as well
as how it should be oriented from its anchored location.
Moreover, whether the extension should be able to share attributes, needs to be
placed at a xed location and the type of extension is specied as attributes for
the extension element.
The XML description for the Extension, containing the level-crossing gure
can be seen in Listing 8.2, and as seen it will utilize the provided gure, dened
in Listing 8.1.
Listing 8.2: Extension for a level-crossing
<config : Extensions width="50"
o r i e n t a t i o n="Center" type="
level −crossing "
f i g u r e=" level −crossing " extension="
ontrack " height="30" fixed=" f a l s e " share=" true ">
<extension : XMLElement Name=" levelCrossing ">
<railxml : Attribute name=" id " value=" id " /
>
<railxml : Element Name=" crossedTrack ">
<railxml : Attribute name=" r e f " value="
track " />
</ railxml : Element>
</ extension : XMLElement>
<extension : Tool></ extension : Tool>
</ config : Extensions>
The extension denition in Listing 8.2 also provides information on how it should
be saved when one chooses to run the verication. When dening how to save
an element, there are special key-words that enables one to store specic infor-
mation. In the case for the level-crossing, we utilize the attribute ref, with
the value set to track. This will say to the XML Handler, that it should store
the ID for the track-section that the Extension is placed at, as the value for
attribute.
When both Listing 8.2 and Listing 8.1 is appended to the conguration in Ap-
==PAGE:154==
140
Results
pendix E, we get the result shown in Figure 8.7.
Figure 8.7: Level-crossing added to the conguration le  shown in the envi-
ronment
In Figure 8.7, we have highlighted the dierent aspects that was added by ex-

both a tool, enabling one to insert level-crossing  and the graphical exten-

The dened level-crossing, however does not show all features that can be

pletely implemented in the model-checker, this was not deemed necessary to

But as one can see, it is easy to dene a new element to be incorporated into
the environment.
It is done by editing an existing conguration le, and if
one has an XML editor, that enables one to follow the XML schema, it is a
straight-forward approach of editing the given conguration.
8.3
Testing
Trough the development of the environment, unit testing was not deployed. This
is due to the fact that the implementation is based heavily on the graphical
front-end, and it was therefore deemed unnecessary to utilize any unit-testing.
However the environment has been thoroughly tested by testing each function-
ality as it has been deployed  which has been done by simply testing whether
==PAGE:155==
8.4 Known Issues
141
the intended work-ow was in place and try some weird choices to see if any
problems would occur.
This approach however is very cumbersome, as one manually has to run the
test each time. This is however always an issue, when testing a graphical user
interface of a program, since it is hard to make an automated test. Having
automated GUI tests could be a solution to solve the manual work-load related to
this kind of testing, however one would still have to analyse the results generated
by the automated GUI tests.
However the manual approach, provided us with the work-ow, that one would
simply test a functionality and x any potential bugs, as they were found. This
means that we would not get tons of bugs at once to work on. This can also be
seen in Section 8.4, where some known and unxed bugs are highlighted.
8.4
Known Issues
Even though we have a functional environment, that solves the task that was
issued, we still have some unsolved issues regarding the solution.
These are
issues, that has been discovered, but due to shortage of time, has not been
resolved.
8.4.1
Zooming
In the current state, it is not advised to zoom in or out when working with
the network layout. There are some issues regarding how the coordinates work
when zoomed in, that has not been resolved.
Ultimately if, one decides to zoom-in on the network-diagram, he may select
dierent elements than where he clicks. The issue lies with the tools, as they
are tted to work with altered coordinates.
A solution would be to get the zoom-value from the workbench and resolve
all coordinates according to this value. This solution was tried as a quick x,
however there were still some issues that was not resolved, and ultimately it was
no xed, due to a shortage of time.
==PAGE:156==
142
Results
8.4.2
Undoing after creation
In case one wants to undo the creation of an element (that has labels attached),
it causes an exception, related to the command that needs to be revoked. The
exception thrown generates the pop-up shown in Figure 8.8.
Figure 8.8: Exception thrown when undoing element creation that contains
labels
This causes the whole environment to behave strangely after the exception has
been thrown, i.e. one cannot work with it constructively. It is therefore a serious
problem, as a potential undo may crash the whole environment.
Due to shortage of time, it has not been possible to resolve this issue, however
the problem lies with the labels being added to the network. When we stopped
adding labels to the network upon creation, the problem stopped occurring.
It would therefore be an appropriate place to start, when determining what is
causing this issue.
8.4.3
Anchors not returned to their original position
When one decides to undo a replacement of an anchor, and wishes to undo
this placement, one will end up with the rectangle representing the anchor, still
located at the new position. This is probably caused by the anchor not being
able to fetch the positioning for the old location when the action is revoked.
==PAGE:157==
Chapter 9
Discussion
The development of the environment, has been a very iterative process, in which
a foundation, that could resolve the requirements was established. The founda-
tion, was the base of any further development, however it was changed several
times, as new ideas or realization that it would not solve the problem was de-
tected. This caused us to rethink the structure of the environment several times,
until an appropriate structure was found and implemented.
This iterative process, also meant that we found a lot of new features, that could
be included in the environment, to either add new functionality or simply make
the user experience better. All in all, we had that these new features, was to
extensive to be implemented in the project period. This is mainly due to the
shortage of work-hours available.
We will in the rest of this chapter, list some of these features that was not
implemented, to give an idea on how one could extend the environment for
future projects. In some cases, an idea to how one could implement the feature
is shown.
==PAGE:158==
144
Discussion
9.1
Improvements and further developments
Even though the environment as is, fulls the functional and non-functional
requirement to some extent, there are still several unimplemented features that
would be nice to have, but is not critical to the environment.
These features would make the environment, much nicer to work with and in-
corporate some feature that would increase what can be expressed within the
environment.
Many of these features, has been thought of during both the
design- and the implementation-phase.
This means that we have several improvements and new features that could be
included in the environment, to both increase the usability and functionality.
9.1.1
Inheritance in XML Conguration
In the current state of the XML Schema, it is not possible to inherit features
from an already existing element. This would be a nice feature to incorporate,
as it would ease the time one has to spend while dening a conguration.
Currently one has to dene almost the same element twice, if it e.g. should
be possible to place a point section in two dierent congurations out of the
box. This could be to indicate two dierent instances of where the MINUS would
initially be placed.
If one could inherit from an element, one could create a template for an ele-
ment, that any other element may change some features for. This would greatly
increase the usability of dening new elements for the conguration, and will
decrease the overhead that will exist in the current XML conguration.
9.1.2
Load DSL XML Files
In the current environment, it is not possible to load an already dened network,
that is written in the XML for the DSL. This is as they contain no spatial
information, that can place the component in their desired locations.
An improvement would be to extend the existing XML format for the DSL,
such that it include spatial information. This information would consist of a
coordinate and a dimension (width and height).
==PAGE:159==
9.1 Improvements and further developments
145
Listing 9.1: Intended extension for the current DSL XML
<s p a t i a l
id="T01">
<l o c a t i on x="100" y="225" />
<dimension width="300" height="50" />
<anchor
id="MINUS" l oc at io n="minus1" />
</ s p a t i a l>
As seen in Listing 9.1, we will simply append a spatial elements to the end of
the DSL XML. These new elements will simply have a reference to an ID, that
relates to an object contained in the network, and apply the spatial information
to that object.
Beside the spatial information, the extension would also provide in which lo-
cation, moveable anchors should be placed at  such that we can recreate the
correct look when loaded.
9.1.2.1
Other Approach
Another approach to load an existing DSL XML le, would be to simply try
to calculate where the dierent objects should be located. This is possible as
we have the relations between the object, which can be used as a hint to which
locations each object should have. One could then start by placing one of the
end-points of the network  and place the other objects based on this objects
location.
This approach however may have issues, when determining which positions an
anchor of e.g. a point sections should have. Therefore it would require su-
ciently more work to apply this method, however it would mean that already
created DSL XML les may be loaded directly into the environment without
any modications, which would help, as some networks are already dened in a
DSL XML le.
9.1.3
Incorporate Logging Facilities
Due to shortage of time when developing the environment, error-reporting was
not implemented to a full-extend. This could be improved by incorporating an
logging functionality, that outputs important information to a log le, this could
be in case errors happens within the code.
==PAGE:160==
146
Discussion
This would enable a user to better understand why a certain functionality is not
working as intended.
Finally, several exception that is thrown during run-time, should be passed up
to the graphical user interface, to be shown either as an pop-up or in a separate
window. This would help the user, understand why a certain functionality is not
giving the expected result  as an exception may be thrown in the background.
9.1.4
Support Landscape Printing
In the current state, one can only print in portrait mode. However as networks
tend to be wider than they are tall, it would be desirable to support printing in
landscape mode, enabling one to contain more of a given network on a page.
9.1.5
Improve Work-ow
Some of the currently implemented work-ows are not quite as user-friendly,
as one would like. Especially the work-ows for connecting the dierent track
section together and moving the track sections could use some improvement, as
it is already explained in Chapter 5.
It would therefore be benecial to implement the work-ows, such that they
correspond to the intended work-ows, as described in Chapter 5. This would
increase the usability of the environment, and provide a much more stream-lined
experience  all in all increasing the overall usability. Some of the intended work-
ows however, may cause some frustration for some users, which means that is
should be possible to choose which parts of the intended work-ows should be
active.
9.1.6
Separate States from Instances
Currently the states for each individual object is stored within the object. How-
ever this increases the diculty of extending the objects at a later time  perhaps
due to a change in the conguration.
This means one would potentially have to run through every object, and re-
place/add states that are not part of the new conguration.
==PAGE:161==
9.1 Improvements and further developments
147
A solution for this, would be to have a form of factory, that handles the states.
When an object receives a change in its parameters, it would simply have to
extract the state (for the new values of the parameters) from the factory. This
returned state will then be applied, like it would be in the current state of the
environment.
This factory would then load in the states that is currently stated within the
conguration, at each start-up ensuring that all objects are not containing out-
dated states.
9.1.7
Support for Equations in States
The implemented environment, only has a very basic approach on how to resolve
if a states should trigger. Currently one can only check for singular values, such
as x = 1, and apply several of these by having " | " between the values, i.e.
x = 1|2|3, which will trigger if x = 1, 2 or 3.
This however can become very cumbersome to work with in case several values
has to be entered. Therefore it would be benecial, to both support equations
and functionality to test for equality and inequality.
Having support for this would greatly increase usability, as one can make com-
plex comparisons in a trigger, by using a one-liner. This would also increase the
possibilities for comparing dierent variables in relation to each other.
9.1.8
Support for Loading New Conguration at Runtime
It should be possible to change the conguration to be used by the environment
at run-time, and the selection must be remembered in the future (could be done
by having a cong-le, that states the current set-up for the environment).
However, in the current state, upgrading the elements within a network, is not
well supported, as currently there is no means of upgrading what is contained
within an object when instantiated. One could solve this, by changing the states,
properties etc. for any given object, such that they match the new conguration.
However problems could occur, by changing the congurations, as element-types
may have been deleted, or the graphical appearance of some types has changed.
This would mean that object may potentially have to be deleted in order for
the network to be legal, according to the new conguration.
==PAGE:162==
148
Discussion
All in all there are a lot of issues when changing the conguration-le, both at
run-time and when closed. These issues would have to be solved  such that
each element may be extended in any way possible before we consider applying
the feature that a conguration-le may be loaded during run-time.
9.1.9
Editor for creating Congurations
A nice feature or utility for the environment, would be to have an editor, that is
designed to dene which elements should be available in the environment. The
editor should be able to dene the graphical appearance of all element, as well
as denes states, properties etc.
This would ease the process of creating the conguration, especially to see
whether a dened appearance for an object is what one wanted.
Currently
there is no graphical conrmation that an elements graphical appearance has
been dened correctly, until one loads up the environment, and deploys the
given type.
An editor for dening the graphical appearance, would be especially nice, as it
could help place anchors, and connection points to be used. And one would be
able to play around with the placement, until they are correctly placed.
9.1.10
Support for xed distance
In the current state, one has to dene percentage of a certain distance when
placing either AnchorConnectionPoint or ExtendableLocation. This can give
some issues when the given element is resized  as the given percentage will move
accordingly (potentially getting far away from the intended location). For this
reason it would be nice to have the same functionality as for the labels, namely
that one could state a xed distance the points would have from a reference
point.
This reference point could for the AnchorConnectionPoint, be a corner of the
boundaries of the TrackSection, it is attached to, and for the Extendable-
Location, one of the ends of the VisualSection, that it is attached to.
==PAGE:163==
9.1 Improvements and further developments
149
9.1.11
Value Type for Properties
One feature that is missing from the dierent properties, is the possibility to
dene which type the value should be. If one would dene, which data-type the
properties would have, it would be possible to verify that a given value, actually
corresponds to what is supposed to be stored.
Validation of input would then have to be applied on the labels, in the property
view and any other location, that enables the user to alter the value of a property.
Alternatively a central method could be deployed, that any object may call
to verify whether a given input is correct (could be deployed directly on the
properties).
9.1.12
Incorporate Verication for Network-layouts
In the current state of the environment, one has to call the external tool-chain
to verify if the static properties of the specication is sound.
This could perhaps be incorporated more into the work-ow, such that illegal
networks cannot be established in the rst place. However, incorporating the
whole verication into the environment, would be a bad idea  as extra static
checks, has to be implemented in two places, namely the tool-chain and the
environment. This will provide a lot of overhead if new rules are to be imple-
mented, since it would cause us to have more places to update (and a harder
time ensuring they are explaining the same properties).
However a solution would be to simply have a light-form of verication in the
network (that will handle that the same section is not connected to it self etc.).
And we will utilize the tool-chain to verify all the static properties that a spec-
ication must full.
9.1.13
Highlight errors on Object
Potential errors, that may occur throughout a run, could be highlighted on
the object itself, by utilizing Markers in Eclipse. These allow one to append
information onto a given resource within the workbench (and associate it as an
error).
These markers can occur, as errors in the error-log, which would provide an easy
==PAGE:164==
150
Discussion
way of seeing if any errors has occurred. The errors, to be shown could both
be if a safety property is not fullled, or if the static checker has detected some
static issues in the network.
In all cases, it will be possible to nd a marked resource by simply clicking on
the text to occur in the error-log. This will provide easy navigability when a
network-layout becomes large.
==PAGE:165==
Chapter 10
Conclusion
A graphical domain specic language has been developed to be used with the
holistic method, that currently exist in RobustRailS. This graphical domain
specic language, is extending the existing domain specic language, that has
been dened for the RobustRailS project.
The graphical domain specic language was developed by dening several ap-
proaches, on how one could visualize both the static and dynamic features of
the domain, it should operate within. A discussing on the advantages and dis-
advantages of each suggestion was conducted, such that we would choose the
best suited solution.
When the visual aspects of the graphical domain specic language was dened
and agreed upon, an editor was implemented to support the new language. To
handle the implementation of the environment, multiple frameworks were stud-
ied, to see which would suit best for the development. An issue that was con-
sidered, was that it should be easy to communicate with the existing tool-chain,
as well as have it contained in a single environment.
Due to these require-
ments, it was chosen not to utilize MetaEdit+, but instead use the more general
framework, that is EMF/GMF, for the implementation process.
The environment that has been developed, is implemented as an plug-in for
Eclipse, as we utilize EMF/GMF, and it enables one to easily dene an editor
==PAGE:166==
152
Conclusion
for Eclipse. This was therefore the natural place to incorporate the editor, as
EMF/GMF were chosen as the developing framework. This environment has
been designed to enable easy extendibility, without having a developer alter any
code. Having an easy extendible environment, was a requirement that was put
in place, as new types could be included in the domain specic language that
we rely on.
The extendibility was handled, by utilizing an XML conguration le, that
follows a schema, such that no illegal congurations could be provided for the
environment. XML was chosen for the extendibility, as it does not require any
prior knowledge of the source code for the environment. And any user may
dene new elements, by simply following the provided schema.
Utilizing an
XML conguration, also provides the benet, that one is unable to break the
environment, by providing faulty code (which could happen in case one could
write code to extend the environment).
This makes it impossible to dene
anything, that is not stated within the schema. This is also the limit of the XML
approach, as dening someone not part of the schema, would require additions
to the schema, as well as the environment (to support the extra features in the
schema). This means that a schema would have to be extensive in order to cover
all scenarios.
In the current conguration, all completed elements within the domain specic
language has been incorporated into the environment, granting them a graphical
part, as dened by the graphical domain specic language. The implemented
elements work as was intended, as they provide both the statically and dynam-
ically features that was dened. This means that the environment fulls that
requirements that was put in place, and it can be utilized to visualize the states
in an interlocking system (provided by the model-checker).
When looking back at the choice of utilizing EMF/GMF for the development
framework, we can see that we have achieved the extendibility, that was intended
from the start. In case one would have chosen MetaEdit+ for the development
framework, we would perhaps not have been able to have this kind of extendibil-
ity. The extendibility has mainly been achieved by the fact that EMF/GMF
is built on-top of Java/Eclipse, meaning we can utilize the features that exists
in Java/Eclipse, something one would probably not have been able to do with
MetaEdit+.
==PAGE:167==
Appendix A
Problem Statement
The problem statement, that was written in the start of the project period.
The signalling system throughout the Danish railway system is to be re-
placed with the standardised European Train Control System (ETCS)
Level 2, ultimately removing all visual signals on the train networks.
A crucial part of this new system is the interlocking systems, which
ensure that all train routes set for the railway network are safe. To
verify the correctness of the interlocking systems, the RobustRailS re-
search project, has as one of its activities developed a holistic method
supporting the verication of such interlocking systems.
Verifying a system according to this method involves a number of steps
in which a number of tools are applied. In the rst step a description
(in XML) of the track layout and route tables are created manually.
Then formal safety properties and a formal system model are generated
from these using a generator tool, and in the last step a model checker
is applied to check that the model satises the safety properties. In
case the model checker detects that a safety property can be broken,
a counter example (i.e. a scenario that leads to an unsafe state) will
be generated. The manual creation of the XML descriptions and the
textual presentation of counter examples are not very user friendly.
==PAGE:168==
154
Problem Statement
The goal of this project is to create a user friendly environment for the
tool suite mentioned above such that one with ease can verify dier-
ent interlocking systems. For this purpose a graphical domain-specic
language (DSL) for describing track layouts and route tables should be
designed and an associated editor to support the creation of descrip-
tions in this language and ensuring that they are not illegal should be
created. Besides the editor, the environment must also provide sup-
port for both the model generator and model checker already developed
for the RobustRailS research project. A visualisation component for
showing counter examples at the DSL level should also be developed.
==PAGE:169==
