==PAGE:172==
1
Installation
To use the software, ﬁrst you must install Eclipse and make that it can use Java.
The software has been tested on, and works with Eclipse Luna Service Release
1a (4.4.1) 64-bit (latest release as of 19-01-2015).
When Eclipse is ready (check that you can execute a simple Java program),
install the following extra packages:
• EMF – Eclipse Modeling Framework SDK (ver. 2.10.1)
• Graphical Modeling Framework (GMF) Runtime (ver. 1.8.1)
• Graphical Modeling Framework (GMF) Tooling - Runtime Extensions
(ver. 3.2.1)
Can be installed by using Help →Install New Software, and under Work with,
select Luna - http://download.eclipse.org/releases/luna. Then the packages to
be installed is located under modelling.
For operating system, Linux Ubuntu 14.04, as some features for the environ-
ment, requires Linux to be run.
NB: Ensure that there are no spaces or other odd characters in the full path
for Eclipse. This is as the plug-in utilizes URLs for ﬁnding some paths, which
gets broken with these characters (may break the plug-ins).
1.1
Install the Editor
The installation of the plug-in, consisting of the environment, one has two steps
to take, installing the JAR ﬁles and copy needed ﬁles into the root of the Eclipse
folder.
Installing JAR Files
The graphical interlocking-system editor can be in-
stalled by extracting the JAR ﬁles from the provided zip ﬁle into either dropin
or plugins of the root of the Eclipse folder (the folders are already created by
Eclipse). Currently the solution consists of six JAR ﬁles:
• org.dtu.rail.ﬁlehandling 1.0.0
• org.dtu.rail.interlocking 1.0.0
• org.dtu.rail.interlocking.diagram 1.0.0
• org.dtu.rail.interlocking.edit 1.0.0
• org.dtu.rail.labels 1.0.0
• org.dtu.rail.simulation 1.0.0
and they are all preceded by an arbitrary number, indicating when they were
created.
1
==PAGE:173==
Install Required Files
The solution needs several ﬁles placed in a folder
named Interlocking, that needs to be located in the root of the Eclipse
folder.
Currently the Interlocking folder is provided in the given ZIP ﬁle. And it must
contain 3 folders:
• conﬁguration – contains the conﬁg.xml ﬁle, that states what elements
are present and an XML schema in the folder xmlschema, which must
contain conﬁguration.xsd at minimum.
• icons – may contain icons that is referenced by the plug-in conﬁguration.
• modelchecker – the model checker to be used. This is the existing tool-
chain of RobustRailS. The editor currently uses ixl.kr in the generic
folder, as the generic model. And generated networks will be placed in
conf as automated.xml
When the required ﬁles and JARs are copied to their correct places within
a Eclipse environment, that has all the necessary features mentioned earlier
installed, we have that the Eclipse plug-in is ready to be executed.
Install Required Packages
The model-checker needs to have some packages
installed in Linux, in order to be guaranteed to work.
The packages to be
installed are:
• libxml
• libc++
• boost
If these are installed, one is guaranteed that the model-checker can be execute
without any issues.
Ready Model-Checker for Execution
The model-checker itself will need
to have permission to execute within Linux. This is done running:
chmod +x rtt-dk-ixl
in the folder containing the executable (that is the model-checker).
This is
located within the provided folder interlocking →modelchecker.
2
Views
When the plug-in has been installed, one can start Eclipse, which will result in
an Eclipse as shown in Figure 1. There are no opened ﬁles, and Eclipse has all
the standard view opened.
In order to show the views included in the plug-in, one has to tell Eclipse that
they should ben shown. This is done by selecting Window →Show View →
Other..., as shown in Figure 2.
2
==PAGE:174==
Figure 1: Fresh installation of Eclipse with Interlocking System Editor installed
By doing this, a dialog will be shown, as seen in Figure 3, in which all views
related to the Interlocking System Editor, can be found under the Interlocking
category, as seen in Figure 3.
The Show View dialog currently has three views related to the Interlocking
System Editor.
If they are not present, or there are missing some, it may
indicate that the plug-ins has not been installed correctly (due to an error in
the initialization of them). The three view, that currently has been implemented
are.
• Labels – used to create new labels and order their placement. See 3.2.6
for more details about this view.
• Route – used to construct and maintain the Interlocking Table used. See 4
for more details about this view.
• Simulation – used to run the veriﬁcation of the Interlocking System. See 5
for more details about this view.
3
Editor
In order to use the views mentioned earlier, one will have to ﬁrst construct a
network – which the other views relates to. This is done by creating a new
interlocking system.
3.1
New Interlocking System
Currently one needs a Project for the interlocking system. A simple Eclipse
Project may suﬃce, and can be created by right-clicking the Package Ex-
plorer or selecting File in the top. Here one select New →Project..., and
follows the instruction in the opened dialog.
3
==PAGE:175==
Figure 2: Show view
When a Project is created, one can create the networks needed. The fastest
way to do this, is to right-click a Project, and select New →Other...
,
which opens the dialog shown in Figure 4. Here one selects Interlocking →
Interlocking Diagram, which open a dialog, that lets you name two ﬁles, one
for the diagram and one for the model.
3.2
Network
When the diagram has been created, one may open the ﬁle ending with .inter-
locking diagram, which will open the main editor, as seen in Figure 5.
The is where one constructs the network, that is to be used by the interlocking
system. And as seen in Figure 5, we have a palette full of tools on the right
side. This contains all elements that may be used within the diagram.
The upper parts, placed under Interlocking is the sections that is deﬁned in
the conﬁg ﬁle. It also contain a single extra entry, namely Connection, that
will be used to connect the diﬀerent sections to each other.
The lower parts, i.e. those under Extension, contains the extension one may
place on a section. This include as seen markerboards, but could also if it had
been implemented contain level crossings
4
==PAGE:176==
Figure 3: Open View Dialog
Figure 4: Dialog for creating Interlocking System
3.2.1
Add Sections
Adding a section is easy, as you select one of the options in the right tool-bar,
such as Linear Section or Point Section.
When selected, one may simply click anywhere on the diagram, i.e. the white
area of Figure 5. This will create the element with the default size.
Alternatively, one may hold the mouse down, and draw a box that will determine
the size, the element will take.
Figure 6, shows an example in which two Linear Sections has been added,
utilizing both methods. The left has the default size – meaning it has been
added by a simple left-click, while the right have had its size determined by the
user when deployed.
5
==PAGE:177==
Figure 5: Empty view for Network Layout
Adjust Anchors
One may adjust the conﬁguration that a section has, by
moving the Anchors placed on the border of a section. Moving an Anchor
is quite simple, as one selects an Anchor, and move it around the edge of the
section.
Then the position, that will be used if one releases the mouse, is highlighted
with a blue box, such that one can determine if it is in the right position.
This all depends on, whether the selected Anchor has any valid positions, it
may take other than the position it already has.
Connect Sections
When section are placed – they are not yet attached to
any neighbours. The way, one connects sections is by utilizing the Connection
tool, found under Interlocking of the tool-bar on the right.
This must be applied on two Anchors, where the free Anchors of the network
in Figure 6 is highlighted with a red box in Figure 7. Two of these Anchors,
may then be connected, as long as they are on the opposite side of the Linear
Section.
By connecting the two Anchors in the middle of the network in Figure 7, we
get a network that is completely connected, as seen in Figure 8.
3.2.2
Add Extension
In case, a section is missing one of the Extensions, one can place these. There
are two types of Extensions (that decides where one may place them).
6
==PAGE:178==
Figure 6: Two linear section added to a network
Figure 7: Unconnected section with unused anchor marked
• Free – represent an Extension, that may be placed anywhere on a section
• Locked – represent an Extension, that must be attached to a ﬁxed location
(this is what a Markerboard represent).
In the current state we only have Markerboards deﬁned, which are Locked
and may be placed in deﬁned locations of a section. An example could be if a
Markerboard in Figure 8 was missing, it could be replaced by adding a new
Markerboard to the position.
3.2.3
Delete Elements
Deleting an element, is simple as one selects the elements that needs to be
deleted, and press Delete.
Deleting elements will make them clean up after themselves, such that if a
section is deleted all Extensions connected to it will be deleted as well. And for
the neighbouring sections, they will simply lose their knowledge of the section,
meaning that connection between them will disappear.
7
==PAGE:179==
Figure 8: Connected network, with only end-anchors free
NB: That in the current version of the editor, an error will arise if an section
and its extensions are deleted at the same time through a selection.
3.2.4
Move Elements
As extension and labels are locked to a certain location in relation to a section,
meaning they cannot be moved manually.
The sections may however be moved as one expects. Either as a single selection
or as a collection. This will currently only change their visual location in the
diagram, but may be altered in later version of the software.
3.2.5
Properties
Properties are retrieved from the conﬁguration of a given object – as stated
in the conﬁguration ﬁle. Each element however has some internal properties,
that cannot be altered from the conﬁguration ﬁle.
The most interesting is
the ID, which is the only editable in the ”normal” property view as seen in
Figure 9.
Figure 9: Internal Property View
Changing the ID, will validate that it does not violate an existing ID, and in
case it does one will be informed, as seen in Figure 10.
8
==PAGE:180==
Figure 10: Connected network, with only end-anchors free
Extended Properties
The properties deﬁned in the conﬁguration ﬁle, may
be shown in an extended property view, as seen in Figure 11, if they are deﬁned
to be shown. Moreover it may be possible to edit the given property, if deﬁned,
and the properties are sorted, such that any editable property is shown in the
top of the view.
Figure 11: Extended Property View
This is the view used to see the internal state of each object, by selecting them
in the network.
3.2.6
Label View
The diﬀerent labels assigned to an object – is managed through the Labels View,
seen in Figure 12. Here you may alter the position of existing labels, or simply
add/delete labels.
X Percentage and Y Percentage is the position in relations to the ”box” of the
parent object given as percentage (meaning 50% is half the width or height of
the ”box”). The X Fixed and Y Fixed is just a ﬁxed value the label should
move on the given axis.
Figure 12: Labels View
Editing Property
The property contained in the label, may be altered by
selecting the label in the network and simply type in the new value the property
represented by the label, must have.
9
==PAGE:181==
Add Label
When adding a label, one simply click the ”Add Label” in top
of the Label View. This brings up the dialog shown in Figure 13, in which all
editable properties not yet used for an label is shown – in case there are no free
properties, nothing happens when the button is pressed.
Figure 13: Dialog for adding label
When added, the label will be placed in the top-left corner of the parent, and
must be moved by altering the values for X Percentage, Y Percentage, X Fixed
and Y Fixed, until it is located where one pleases. This is seen in Figure 14,
where all values for the length label is set to zero.
Figure 14: Values for a newly added label
4
Route Table
The routes of the interlocking system, can be found in the Route Table, which is
shown in the Route View, seen in Figure 15. Here you can control which routes
should be veriﬁed for the network drawn in the editor view.
The simulation, see Figure 5, has an option to automatically generate all routes
for you.
10
==PAGE:182==
Figure 15: Routes View
An interlocking table has an automated id, automated routes to start with. This
may be changed by altering the text.
4.1
Add Route
Adding a route is easy, as one simply has to press the button with the ”+”
on, which adds an empty route, with an automatically generated ID, as seen in
Figure 16
Figure 16: Adding route to Interlocking System
One can continuously add more routes, as one pleases, however one needs to
edit the diﬀerent aspects of the route, see 4.3.
4.2
Delete Route
Deleting routes is easy, as one has to check the Delete Entries check-box,
which enable one to delete any route. This is done by simply left-clicking on
any of the routes in the table, and simply delete it whenever the check-box is
checked.
4.3
Modify Route
When one wants to modify a given route-entry, one will simply have to click
on each individual column of the table to modify the given data that is stored
within the column.
11
==PAGE:183==
4.3.1
Changing ID
Changing the ID, is done by pressing the ID coloumn for a given route, which
will prompt the user with the dialog shown in Figure 17.
Figure 17: Dialog for changing an routes ID
In case there already exist a route with the provided ID, one is informed, and
the change cannot happen.
4.3.2
Source and Destination
The source and destination of a route, will be selected from a list in which only
one element may be selected – and will be one of the Markerboards that is
present in the network. In case a source or destination is already chosen, a new
selection will override the old value, when selected from the list.
Figure 18: Dialog for add source to a route (or destination)
As seen in Figure 18, all Markerboards present in the opened network will be
able to be selected, as the source or destination. As a bonus information, one
can identify in which direction the Markerboard is placed.
12
==PAGE:184==
4.3.3
Points
Adding points, needs a unique dialog, as one has to choose which position the
points will have.
For this reason a special dialog is constructed, as seen in
Figure 19
Figure 19: Dialog available for choosing points
One may select multiple points at once, and when one presses ok, they will all
get the conﬁguration that was chosen between the options. Currently there are
two options – p (PLUS) or m (MINUS).
4.3.4
Adding or Deleting Entries
Some columns, i.e. Overlaps, Points, Signals, Path, Level Crossings and Con-
ﬂict all may contain multiple elements of the type that is needed in the given
column.
When adding/deleting entries in these columns one gets a list dialog, as the one
in Figure 18, where one can select multiple entries.
In order to add an entry to a given column, one has to simply left-click the
given cell in the table (that represent the type that one want to add). This
enables one to add multiple entries.
And if one wants to delete any entries from any of the given column, one
simply click the cell, while holding ctrl, and a dialog will be presented with the
current entries that is contained within the cell (all selected entries will then get
deleted).
13
==PAGE:185==
4.4
Saving Routes
In the current state of the environment, routes are not automatically saved
to the interlocking system. This is as a result that one might automatically
generate the routes that will be used – and one might not need to store these
in the interlocking system.
To store the given interlocking table, one must therefore press the Save Routes
button. The saved routes will be loaded whenever the given network is opened.
5
Simulation
The simulation view is where one controls any interaction with the model-
checker. It allows for verifying the solution, generating routes etc. The view
can be seen in Figure 20.
Figure 20: Simulation View
The simulation view consists of multiple parts, in which we have:
• Tool-bar – contains everything that controls the model-checker and visu-
alization of potential errors generated from this.
• Results – include all the variables for each element, when a counter-
example is presented.
• Invariance – show the result of each invariant that the model-checker has
checked for.
• Errors – contain any errors reported from the model-checker, this could
be caused by having a semantically incorrect network etc.
5.1
Tool-bar
This contain several button and other input ﬁelds, that is used to control the
model-checker or visualization of the network in various ways.
5.1.1
Current Step
The current step, seen in Figure 21, controls which step in the counter-example
should be visualized on the current network. In case no counter-example exists
14
==PAGE:186==
this will not give any result.
Figure 21: Current Step
It can be controlled in two ways:
• Use arrows – using the arrows will either decrease (left) or increase (right)
the current step by one.
• Typing – one may type a step into the text-ﬁeld, which will show the
counter-example for that step or the nearest legal step.
5.1.2
Conﬁguration
The conﬁguration control what will be sent to the model-checker and when to
communicate with the model-checker.
We have three buttons:
• Generate Route – will generate all routes that relates to the opened net-
work (these will be parsed into the routes view).
• Verify Solution – will verify the solution in any given way, i.e. both stati-
cally to see if it is wellformed or if it is sound. This will generate counter-
examples if the number of selected steps are more than 0, and we have
that the network fail some of the safety properties.
• Reset State – this will reset the visual representation of the state on the
network (in case counter-example has been provided). This will return
each element to its initial state.
Use own Routes
Beside the buttons, one also has the option to use his own
route, which is simply done by checking of the Use own routes, which means
that the model-checker will get the routes deﬁned in the route view.
Induction Steps
At last one will have to select the number of induction steps
that the model-checker must perform before claiming that the network is sound.
This is done with the spinner Number of simulation steps. NB: note that it
may take a long time if this number is large
5.2
Result View
Here every state-variable will be listed in case counter-examples are generated
when verifying the interlocking system.
15
==PAGE:187==
Figure 22: Result shown in the result view
The elements contained in this table is sorted alphabetically (according to their
ID), such that it is easy to ﬁnd a given element. In Figure 22, the ﬁrst step of
the counter-example is chosen, which when applied to the given network, result
in what can be seen in Figure 23
One may collapse a given elements attributes, by clicked the triangle next to
the identiﬁer.
5.3
Invariant View
The invariant view will simply display the result of each safety property, that
has been checked for during the veriﬁcation. An example of this view ﬁlled out
with results, can be seen in Figure 24.
5.4
Error View
The error view is responsible for giving the user information about any errors
that happened during the execution of the model-checker. This could be er-
rors from the static checker – in case violation on the structure of the network
happens.
And example of this can be seen in Figure 25, in which it is stated that an
section has no neighbours.
16
==PAGE:188==
Figure 23: Result from counter-example applied to the network
Figure 24: Invariant result from the counter-example shown in invariants view
